They retrieved materials and supplies from crates located on the floor, walls, and ceiling, though it was hard to tell which surface was which. A crew member loaded a cluster of missiles into one of the largest ships in the bay, while another checked and calibrated dark matter receptacles that were slated for maintenance.
Alan fit the Umbra on one of the middle decks, between a freighter and an exploratory craft. With a few feet to either side, Alan parked it in the snug space.
The engines powered down as Alan locked the ship, restraining it with cables that attached from the bay. While the crew was gone, these cables would also run diagnostics, supplement missing raw materials and supplies, while the small, four-legged, mechanical repatons would scurry about and perform minor fixes.
He headed into the back of the Umbra where Aki was already waiting. The medbed had healed a large part of the wound, but Argus was still unconscious. The two of them hoisted Argus up onto their shoulders. The large man's armor had already been removed and lay to the side in pieces, but they still needed all of their strength to get him out of the ship and out into the docking bay.
Alan breathed a sigh of relief as they entered the zero-g. He pushed off the side of the Umbra and guided them with the railing. A pair of orderlies wearing white uniforms stood nearby and hurried over to Argus. They grabbed his arms and torso and deposited him into the floating bed. He groaned as they shifted his leg into position. A translucent green cover came out from one side of the medbed and covered Argus in a protective dome, displaying his vital signs. Alan watched the little wave move up and down. The man's heart beat strongly.
"He'll be in the sector two medical bay if you'd like to see his recovery, Second Medium," an orderly said. He took a quick glance at the chart beamed atop the cover. "Most of it has healed, so it shouldn't take more than a few hours to get him functional again."
"Take a look at his levels and supplement anything he might be missing. I want him in top shape," Alan said. He hoisted his supplies onto his shoulder as the orderly ran calculations.
"As you wish, Second Medium." The two orderlies pushed off, bringing Argus with them. They dodged and wove their way through the web made from the stream of crew and supplies, moving with skill honed from years of practice, until they reached a lane that led to the bay doors. Alan and Aki followed in their wake.
As the orderlies were about to leave the bay, they paused, set the medbed onto the floor and bowed low to the staircase above them. Though he couldn't see her, Alan knew who was coming.
All the mechanics, welding and tinkering, holstered their tools. Crew that floated in midair with boxes of equipment between them became quiet and spun to face the doorway. It seemed like even the machines had stopped their humming.
A guard barked out, "High Adjudicant on deck!" The guard bowed his head down, his face stern and his posture unyielding.
The docking bay had become silent. Alan heard the firm footfalls of boots with hard heels striking against the floor.
Claudia Einhart was a sharp woman, both in mind and body. The drab, green military uniform that was standard for the soldiers of the Einhart was tailored into a suit for her tall frame. It hung upon her shoulders and cinched in at her slender waist, making her seem more masculine than she was. Though she was the High Adjudicant of the Einhart, the only decoration she allowed herself was a cravat with a jeweled wave pinned to it, the stylized crest of the Einhart house, tucked under her vest. Golden hair flowed out behind her, reaching almost to her knees. It was pulled tight against her head, kept tied back with a small black ribbon, though bangs still stuck out over her forehead. As she entered the dock, the long braid took a life of its own in the absence of gravity, undulating behind her.
Upon her angular face were two green eyes that scanned the room in an instant, taking in and etching everything into her memory. Alan looked into those eyes and felt as if they could see into his past, and he almost lowered his own out of shame. But he held his head high. He and Aki were the only ones to do so.
Claudia nodded once and the dock came back to life as people returned to their duties. Some people were slow to resume work; their gazes lingered on the High Adjudicant.
"Welcome home, Alan," Claudia said. Her voice was commanding yet soft, requiring no volume to assert authority.
"It's good to be back," Alan said. He held a big black case that contained his equipment, and another duffel bag that had the rest of his supplies from the mission.
"Walk with me," Claudia said. She turned around and pushed herself back into the doorway, her feet landing with two clicks upon the floor.
Alan nodded to Aki. She went back into the ship to get her own belongings. Near the door, he drifted to a translucent box, scratched and dented with years of use, and placed his items inside it. Affectionately called "the pipe" by everyone aboard the Aurelius, it served as the internal transport system of the capital ship, using pressure and vacuum to deliver packages and other goods throughout. Alan scanned his hand on a nearby panel. After confirming the location of his living quarters, a lid closed down on the box. His belongings were sucked into the tube and disappeared.
He turned and entered the hallway, bending his knees to absorb the shock as the gravity pulled him to the floor. Claudia was a few steps ahead of him and he hurried to catch up to her. A part of her ponytail brushed against his arm as he kept pace with her.
They made a right through a corridor that seemed identical to the one they had just been in, the gray metal walls decorated with a continuous knot that looked like a long ocean wave, the crest of the Einhart. Anyone they met quickly stuck their backs to their wall and held their heads down in respect. After passing a few more people, they strode together in silence into a small compartment, with buttons on the side designating different areas of the ship. Claudia ignored all the buttons and pressed her hand against the side of a compartment wall, where hidden sensors scanned her biometrics. The lights inside turned blue, bathing them in an aqua glow. The lift started moving, powered by supercooled magnets that pushed against railings that lined most areas of the ship.
When it was just the two of them, Claudia breathed out and relaxed her shoulders. She turned to Alan. "One can never be too careful, the walls have ears and they hear what they want," she said. 
"How was the last excursion to find Sanctuary?" Alan asked.
"A failure," she said, recalling the reports. "I've stopped watching the live feed and just wait for the results now."
"What happened?"
"An ambush happened. We lost another three ships on a false tip. Not one of the scouts made it back."
"We've always had scouts to spare."
"Three more families to contact, three more souls to commemorate." Claudia looked at Alan. "We have enough support and resources, but we need to put them in the right place. I want an end to this fruitless search."
"So do I," Alan responded. "It's like chasing a shadow in darkspace." He gazed out at sea of stars. "I wonder if we'll ever find the throneworld."
"We will. But right now, I'd like some good news. What did you see on Galea?"
Alan fished inside one of his pockets for a small green chip and handed it to Claudia. "This is what we saw," he said.
"I wish I could've seen this earlier, but with Sanctum trackers all over darkspace it's good we waited." She inserted the chip into her wristband and it projected an image in front of them.
"These were the recordings from the Umbra. When you contacted me, a giant tree had just destroyed the Javelin-class warship, the Edict," Alan said.
Claudia nodded. "I saw from our ships on the other side of the Galea. We did what we could to evacuate the population there, but when we arrived the empire was mostly finished. They didn't leave much to rescue."
"It must've been serious if you participated."
"I was linked with the Aurelius and coordinated rescue efforts from here; it's easier when there's only one person directing. But I'm more interested in what happened over their capital city."
They watched the video together.
"The Edict had already begun to vaporize the remnants of the city when there was an explosion from inside the ship," Alan explained, pointing out areas of the battle. "After that, there was a small white dot; you can see it here, almost like a seed. It fell to the bottom of the ship, where it split and a tree started to grow out of it." He followed it with his finger in the projection as it fell. "At this point, it appears the ship shielding also failed."
"What did the tree do?" she asked.
"It went through all the armor and destroyed the ship in seconds."
She watched the light tree grow its way through the Edict. "Seems like the Edict had no defense against it."
"None. In moments, this tree blew through plating that normally would've required the full armory of three of our warships to get through. The strange thing is, when it happened, the Umbra didn't detect any heat signatures. Seemed like whatever the light went through just disappeared. And when it was finished, it just vanished. It left the Edict so full of holes it collapsed on itself."
The video ended as the compartment came to a halt with a ding. "I'll send this over to engineering to have it looked at further," Claudia said.
They stepped out. Two wooden doors closed behind them, and Alan could feel the slight change in pressure behind him as the compartment left. He found himself standing on a crest of the Einhart carved out of marble. The ceilings were low, for space was always at a premium on the Aurelius, but it was luxuriously furnished. He whistled appreciatively.
"You've been away too long," Claudia said as she walked, allowing herself a brief smile that no one would see.
A butler stood by and opened the door. It was old-fashioned, complete with a brass knob. Alan couldn't remember the last time he had seen it. The butler bowed and asked, "Will the High Adjudicant be having company for dinner tonight?" 
Alan was suddenly aware of how dirty his clothes were and just how much dust from Galea still clung to him. He felt as if he were sullying the room by just being there.
"I-" he started to say, but Claudia held up a hand and silenced him. 
She looked at the butler. "Set the table for two," she said. The butler nodded and disappeared.
They walked into the dining area, where a tablecloth was draped over a heavy granite table bolted to the floor. As she walked past it, Claudia let her fingers trail across the linen.
Alan knew that this table had seen the highest level of negotiations between the Einhart and the worlds that sought their protection. Decisions that saved and damned countless people were made here, and alliances were forged on this surface, some of which were broken later.
And once, when Alan was away on a mission, an assassin managed to gain entry into Claudia's chambers. Alan watched the recording-after a brief fight that involved broken plates on the floor and sauces on the walls, Claudia threw the assassin onto the table and pulled back the mask that protected his neck. She grabbed the knife she had been using, still with bits of vegetables and meat stuck to it, and pulled it across his throat. The tablecloth had absorbed most the blood so cleanup hadn't taken long.
Claudia took a seat at the head of the table and gestured to the chair on her left, pulling Alan out of his memories. He went to the chair and sat. Alan sank into the rich upholstery, so different than the fabricated command chair he was used to sitting in. The butler scurried around them, placing utensils of different shapes and sizes in rows next to china that was decorated with the symbol of the Einhart on its rims, the continuous knot winding and flowing across its surface. He picked up one of the utensils. It looked like a fork with three tines, except the ones on the sides were bent outwards. 
Claudia saw him studying it. "It's for variety," she said. The butler set down a plate of meats and soft cheeses, some of which Alan had never seen before. Claudia poked one of the prongs into a thin slice of marbled meat, and another into some kind of pungent leaf. She put both tines into her mouth and appeared to savor the flavors. Alan mimicked her.
"There is an art to putting things together in combinations that one might not suspect are good at first," she said. "With three tines, you have the choice of what you want, and an extra prong, for the freedom to experiment."
The meal continued with a root plant that took the shape of a fractal, colors breaking all over its surface and spreading across tiny points, giving Alan the impression that he was eating glass. But when it touched his tongue it dissolved into something that was so soft and buttery he barely had to chew. Alan wanted to stick his tongue out to see what color it had turned, but he resisted the urge.
In between bites of a meat he didn't know the name of from a planet he hadn't heard of, he asked, "Are you worried? We don't know what's inside the escape pod we brought back to the Aurelius. It could be a weapon that the empire has been dying to get aboard our ship."
Claudia put her spoon down. "That's what the earlier scans were for. We couldn't confirm if there was a life form inside it, so engineering has the pod stored away in a remote and detached bay normally reserved for experiments. If it's a new weapon that the empire has developed and we have it, it may just be what we need. Who knows, it might even lead us to Sanctuary."
"Wouldn't that be nice," Alan said. "Bringing the fight to them. Still, I'm more concerned with our performance on Galea."
"What bothers you? You did as you were ordered and performed well, as always."
"We arrived late. If we had gone earlier-"
Claudia held up a hand to dismiss his train of thought. "That was the choice of their Erudite and the rules he followed to govern his people. We did what we could in the time we were given." She looked him in the eyes. "You can't save them all, Alan." She took another sip of her soup, the spoon barely touching her lips. "Speaking of which, I heard you brought one of them back."
Alan nodded. He suddenly found the seasonings inside the soft and creamy pile of mashed vegetables very interesting and didn't look up.
"You know it is against regulation to take on soldiers without proper training," Claudia continued. "We need to make sure he is dedicated to our cause, that he is a good fit for the Einhart before we let him fight our fight."
Alan nodded again. "But he was also one of the Erudite's guards," he said. "He held Seth at bay for a few seconds, which is more than what most soldiers of other conquered worlds could say. Please Claudia, let me have this one."
She thought for a moment. "Simmons would be unhappy," she said, though her eyes betrayed the mirth behind her words. "At least five broken rules."
"All the more reason to do it," Alan responded. She chuckled at his remark. "I don't know why you made him a Second Medium as well," Alan said.
"He has his strengths and weaknesses, just as you do Alan."
"When I spoke to him on Galea, he told me the AIs chose not to pursue Seth. Did they really decide against destroying the Prince?" he asked. 
"Two out of the three did. Atropos took your side, as she always has," she said. "But because of our rescue efforts, Galea, or what is left of it, is now our ally. Their people will support us and our cause. It wouldn't be good if it were known we killed their leader. It's better to use his death as a tool against the empire."
"But it was the First Prince, the Voice himself. He was right there above me."
"I'm not sure even destroying his vessel could've killed Seth," Claudia said. "From what we know, he is the result of three merged princes and is still in one piece mentally. Who knows what he can sustain?"
Alan let the matter drop. What was past was past, and soon Alan forgot about it. They moved on to the final course of the meal, a clear jelly that was suffused with the essence of a plant Alan couldn't pronounce.
"Though we can't compete with the empire in terms of conscripted men, what we lack in numbers we make up for with passion," Claudia said. "There are those here that are willing to fight, like you, when you first arrived from Cybele. The academies are full; they've gotten so large we've had to split them into multiple satellites. There wouldn't be any space to train?what's his name?"
"Argus Volgren."
"Another A name. It'll be hard to keep you all apart."
"You wouldn't need to if we were together as a team," Alan said, pushing the point.
"I do have to commend you in some way, and I can't promote you because you're right below me?"
"The only thing I would like is Argus," Alan said.
She debated as she sipped the jelly from a small glass. "Very well," she said. "I trust your judgment." Even Alan was flattered by the highest praise Claudia could give. "He will have access to information pertaining to you, Aki and your missions. But if he appears as a threat to the Einhart in any-"
"He won't," Alan said. "He is skilled and thoughtful. And if he's going to be a problem, well, I would be the first to correct my mistake."
Claudia nodded. "We are agreed."
They finished the rest of their meal in silence. Precisely one hour after he had entered, Claudia bid him farewell. The butler escorted him to the pipe where a lift was waiting for him. When he arrived in his own apartment, he took two more Virifen pills and entered an empty home.

Chapter 7

Argus found him where he thought he'd find him; half-naked and sprawled across a small clearing on the ground, an empty bottle lying next to him. Argus knelt and shook Alan gently with his hand, but he didn't stir from his slumber. Alan only cradled the bottle between his arms, the moonlight reflecting off the green glass. 
Argus lied down next to him, his body barely fitting within the cramped space. He looked up at the sky, illuminated by one moon that was falling toward the horizon, its brilliance bathing a field of wheat in a hazy glow, making it look like an ocean trembling under the weight of its own waves. He studied the stars, stars in positions he had never seen before, and when he closed his eyes he could almost feel the breeze that wound through the golden stalks around him. And for the first time since he had left Galea, Argus felt a sense of who he used to be. He dug into his memory and recited:

"I have always preferred the night
with its gentle slopes
leading to steep cliffs,
Seeing things only I can see.
Before sunlight 
melts my dreams."

It was from the Book of Rudera, a poet he had found and come to like during his studies on Galea. Alan stirred from his slumber and opened one sleepy eye to gaze at the large man next to him in the wheat field.
He yawned. "Am I dreaming?" he asked sleepily. His voice was gravelly and his breath was tinged with spirits.
"If you were, I'd be flattered," Argus responded.
Alan's smile cracked the dried trail of saliva that ran down his chin. He turned until he faced the sky, the moonlight making his skin look paler than it was, covering the scars on his chest and arms in a white blanket. "I was supposed to go see you in the med bay," he said. "You weren't supposed to come see me." His words were muffled as he rolled over into a pillow. He didn't remember pulling it off the couch while he slept; must've been out of habit.
Argus continued to gaze at the moon. "It's amazing how fast we can heal with the right tools." 
"Yeah, you'll be pissing out nanodocs for a week," Alan said.
"I had a chance to read up on those machines while I was recovering. Fascinating what they can do." There was no pain when Argus flexed his leg. They could've removed the scar, but he wanted to keep it as a reminder of his own failure. The largest civilian clothes they had in the med bay ripped at the seams as soon as he pulled them over his head, but thankfully, a uniform and a set of field garments in his size had been delivered to his new room, along with the location of Alan's living quarters. Argus had changed into his new military garb, wearing the dark green of the Einhart. He enjoyed the feeling of the uniform. It gave him a sense of belonging, even though it was tight around the chest and the colors were different from what he was used to. 
"You look at the stars as if you've never seen them before," Alan said from the pillow.
"They really are beautiful. So different from what we had on Galea," Argus said. "The city lights made it impossible to see them like this." He tried to find the animal constellations he had heard of. When he only saw the skyline of the city he missed in the stars, he made up some of his own, connecting imaginary lines between the spheres scattered across the sky.
Alan turned and stretched. "But from what I saw, there was always something to do in the evenings in Galea." Alan looked at the expanse of wheat surrounding them. "Back on Cybele, all we could do was lay down and look up. See which one of us could get off the rock first; which one of us could get rid of the smell of dirt from our clothes the fastest."
"It's funny how much we think we know when we're younger," Argus said.
Alan propped himself up on his elbows. He pointed to a blip in the distance. A small light sat atop the fields, barely distinguishable from the sea of yellow. "I was born there. Grew up on that farm. You wouldn't believe the stink."
Argus looked at where he pointed. "If you could go back, would you?"
Alan remained silent and gazed at the light. He could make out the faint outline of the house that surrounded it, but there his memory blurred and so did the image, and beyond it was nothing more than a haze. He remembered the musk of the livestock and a big red barn that he had played in. But its size and shape had been lost along with his childhood.
Alan turned to look at Argus. "Why are you here?" he asked.
Argus sat up. "The High Adjudicant is ready to open the pod we brought back."
"Time?" Alan asked. 
"In thirty minutes. One of the scientists was concerned when you couldn't be reached, so I came to fetch you. Though he seemed more worried that the High Adjudicant would ask him where you were."
Alan stood up, balancing on one leg since the other had gone numb. He reached out into the wheat field and pressed his finger down on an invisible button in the air. The field, moon, and stars around them flashed and receded, swallowed up by the holographic display in the center of the room. It was a small cube that took up little space on Alan's one table. All it left behind were empty walls and memories.
"I'll be waiting outside," Argus said.

Chapter 8

Fifteen minutes later, the engineer sighed in relief as Alan strode into view with Argus behind him. They were in a testing facility, a module separate from the Aurelius created as a containment unit for things that were deemed too unsafe to bring onto the ship itself. Argus watched as the docking tube that brought them here retracted back into the main body of the ship and left them floating alone.
Claudia was already there, standing in the center and back. Surrounding her were a dozen guards that were spread out across the room, their faces stern and their guns held in firm grips. In the row of control panels that lined the thick windows, Aki sat at a light console with the other engineers. They viewed charts and graphs that had lines bouncing up and down them as sensors measured particle and wave concentrations. The engineers wore white uniforms similar to the medics, except for a green stripe that ran across their arms and backs. 
Another engineer was calibrating the mechanical arms for use inside the test chamber. Two giant metal hands stretched from the ceiling, connected to the chamber with purple electrical tethers similar to the ones used by the engines of the Umbra. They shifted position slightly as the engineer raised his hands and light projected onto his hands and forearms, tracing the contour and position of his extended arms. He flexed his fingers. Though they were hundreds of times larger than his hand, the robotic digits inside the testing chamber made the same movements he did. The hiss of the hydraulics came through the speakers. He lifted his arms and the robotic counterparts followed, first from the floor to the ceiling. With a tap of his foot, he locked them into place and awaited orders.
As they walked toward the assembled group, Alan gave Argus a black square patch that looked like a sticker. "Just in case of depressurization," he said. Argus didn't see how a synthetic patch that fit in the palm of his hand could protect him from space, but he kept it in his pocket and saved his questions for later. Claudia glanced at them as they arrived.
An engineer turned around. "Preparations are ready, ma'am."
Claudia nodded. "Bring the capsule into the quarantine zone," she said.
A set of blast doors opened, revealing stars and nebulas behind it. Still tethered to the outside of the testing facility was the escape pod, its silver hull battered and blackened. The engineer manipulated the mechanical hands. He guided the fingers and palms around the capsule and locked them in place. The tethers decoupled from the capsule and retracted as the two hands guided it inside. The blast doors closed and the testing chamber repressurized with a blast of air.
"Object is secure, ma'am," he said. 
"Wave concentrations are within normal ranges," another reported.
"Check for outbound transmissions," Claudia said.
The observation deck turned dark. Scanners mounted on the walls of the chamber swept waves of red light across the capsule and everything else in the room. "There are residual darkspace signatures," the engineer reported, "suggesting it has some kind of generator onboard, but other than that, it's clear, ma'am."
"Open it," she said.
Two digits separated themselves from the hands and probed the surface for a latch. They clicked and tapped and pulled at anything that stuck out, but nothing happened. When they could find no way to open it, the engineers whispered among themselves. 
One brave engineer spoke up. "Ma'am, the vessel has been tampered with. The normal opening mechanisms for this kind of craft are not there," she said. "We normally look for a latch that can be accessed manually from the outside, but this one doesn't seem to have any such device."
"Find one," Claudia said.
Thick wires came down from the ceiling and the walls, anchoring the capsule into place, holding it like an orb in the middle of a web. The hands split into ten separate fingers, each one controlled by an engineer. The digits hovered over the surface, each trying to find an access port.
They still couldn't find anything after a few more minutes of searching. With each passing moment more beads of sweat grew on the engineers' foreheads as they kept the High Adjudicant waiting. Most knew that she was not a patient woman, and those that didn't know soon found out. She began tapping one foot and the engineers doubled the speed of their commands.
While they worked, Aki continued scanning the vessel. She overlaid image upon image, pieced together data from the graphs, and realized something. "It's waiting for an input," she said. "Since there isn't any physical interface, I'm assuming it has to be wave-based." She set a wave routine into the computer by holding her thumb down on a button on her console and selecting the pattern with her free fingers. 
"If I may," she said, glancing at the engineers. They stopped reluctantly. "Thank you. Commencing bombardment." She had the computer cycle through various combinations of waves, from infrared to ultraviolet, sound to pressure. The quarantine chamber darkened as light waves from the visible spectrum were removed. After that, the chamber glowed a rainbow of colors as different waves hit the escape pod and bounced off. But none seemed to work. A couple engineers snickered but a stern look from Alan and they were quiet again.
"Computer, display the waveform observed on Galea. The delta waves," Aki said. A series of jagged peaks and valleys traced in blue appeared. "Replicate and bombard," she said.
There it was. A slight dip in the readings as the ship absorbed them. But it still didn't open.
"Delta waves absorbed," she said. "We're getting closer."
"The human brain wave?" an engineer asked. 
Aki nodded. "But the computer recreated it through a spatial medium, changing its innate properties. And it requires a whole host of other triggers it seems." She turned and looked at Claudia. "We need someone to go down there."
The engineers shuffled uneasily in their chairs, more focused on their consoles than ever before. If and when the pod opened, no one knew what would come out of it. The empire had played such tricks before. Whole stations had been destroyed by a well-hidden bomb or by biologics kept in stasis. And it was strange that an escape pod would have a darkspace drive attached, normally reserved for more important ships.
Alan walked over to the door that led down to the quarantine zone. His footsteps were the only sound that echoed in the observation deck. Everyone turned to look at him. He placed his hand on the pad next to the door, but the door didn't budge. "Open the door," he said. 
The engineers were unsure and looked to Claudia for guidance. "Ma'am?" one asked.
"I'm tired of waiting," Alan said. "Plus, I brought the damn thing back." He pressed his hand against the pad for emphasis, feeling the gel rebound with each tap of his palm.
Claudia looked at him. "I trust your judgment, Second Medium," she said. She nodded to the engineer and he unlocked the door.
Alan went through and Argus followed close behind. With a hiss, the door closed behind them. The air was cooler inside the quarantine zone, still not completely heated after repressurization. Alan saw his breath drifting over him as his feet beat out a staccato down the stairs. The escape pod looked larger now that he was standing in front of it. The fingers of the mechanical hand still swarmed over it.
"Can you get these off?" he asked, never taking his eyes away from the escape pod. Argus snorted once, and Alan ignored it.
The fingers scuttled away and reformed themselves into a hand connected by electric ligaments. It moved to the side of the room, leaving Alan to work with the capsule.
"Get ready," he said. Argus pulled out a pistol from his holster and aimed it on what was assumed to be the door.
Alan moved closer and put one hand on its surface, feeling the dents in the metal. He drew closer and pressed his ear against the door with no windows. He heard a click come from inside. 
"Pod is decompressing," Aki said through the speakers.
Alan backed away and brought out his own gun. Steam poured out of the small vessel. He barely had time to react as the door fell forward from the mist. He jumped backwards, and it landed where he had been standing a moment before.
"Jump in delta waves!" an engineer said. Alan looked up to see thick steel plates fall from the ceiling. They shielded the observation deck and blocked his view of what happened above him. He felt a wave of disorientation, as if he were on the verge of sleep and had the sensation of falling. He tapped his foot on the floor to rid himself of the feeling. An iridescent sheen washed over the metal slats as they absorbed the waves.
The ventilation system engaged. The mist turned into white funnels that were sucked into the ceiling. As the steam disappeared, Alan faced the plush red interior of the pod. In front of the pod was a small figure. She had a mass of white hair that floated around her as if she were underwater. The dress that billowed around her was once white, but now it was stained with blood and dirty with grime. On the floor of the escape pod, a small object glistened underneath its cloth covering. He quickly scooped it up and put it into one of his pockets before the mist completely cleared.
When Alan saw her face, he felt something stir deep inside his memories. He wished he had a couple capsules of Virifen, but he had forgotten them in his room. He patted his pockets in vain with his free hand as he walked closer.
Though he did not know how, the girl was floating as if there were no gravity in the chamber. Trusting Argus, he holstered his gun and then put his arms under her, afraid she might fall at any moment. When his forearms touched the edges of her dress, her hair fell straight down followed by the rest of her body. Alan easily caught and held her.
She was tiny, or perhaps it had been too long since Alan had been near a child. The rapid movements under her eyelids stopped. Alan heard the shutters rise behind him, but he didn't turn around. There was something about this girl that was familiar, but he didn't want to see it. He felt a tingle in the back of his mind. Argus shifted uncomfortably on his feet, his gun still held steady. He felt the tingle too.
Like the memory of a spring breeze, Alan felt different parts of his mind respond to the strange feeling, as if a gentle scent had enveloped his thoughts. But there were no words, only a jumble of emotions. There was fear and confusion. Underlying everything, a current of sadness swept over it all like night on an ocean.
Claudia opened the com and her voice came through the speakers. "Alan, what is it?"
Alan paused, unsure himself. "It's a girl, Claudia," he responded. "Just a little girl." His voice was calm.
"Is it a threat?"
"I?I don't know." He looked down at the girl who was now shivering in his arms from the cold. "I don't think so." A tear slipped past one of her eyelids. He swept it away with a gloved hand, watching it leave a glistening trail across her cheek.
This time an engineer spoke. "Ma'am, if I may. We don't know what that thing is. It could be a Sanctum trick. We need to keep it under observation, find out what we can, then dispose of it in a timely manner. It may compromise the location of the Aurelius and bring the empire upon us." Alan wasn't supposed to hear him, but he could imagine Aki accidentally keeping a finger on the com switch as they debated above him. He was glad he had an ally up there.
"She needs help." Alan whispered, but he knew the microphones were sensitive. "She's been chased by the empire. Who knows what happened to her before we brought her here?"
"She was clearly important to the empire, and now we have her," Claudia said. "But she still may pose a threat to the Einhart, and until we can determine she is not one, she will remain here, under close observation."
Alan nodded. "Bring in a medbed," he said. He turned and looked at the opaque glass of the observation deck. "And warm this damn room up!"

Chapter 9

Many in the empire looked down on Ashra. Most knew her as the strange child that was always with her mother, Lydia, a woman who kept to herself and had few, if any, friends. And though Ashra was the daughter of the emperor, she was also the daughter of a trophy of war. Aboard the Edict, Ashra remembered the times when others had asked her questions, something as simple as her name, and she couldn't respond. She could only tilt her head towards the source of the sound, with her eyes closed and her ears open. 
Once, when Lydia wasn't there to protect her, a few of the children on the Edict had cornered her. She remembered their damp palms on the sides of her face, she recalled being held back and forced to face them and answer their questions and taunts. She didn't know what the effect was, but when she opened her eyes the bullies released their grasp and backed away. 
When Ashra looked at them with her eyes with no irises, no pupils, just a blank white canvas that stretched between her eyelids, the other children didn't know how to respond. Unnerved, they released their grip and ran. They ran back to their parents working aboard and they told them of the deaf, mute, and blind girl that roamed the halls of the ship like a ghost. Ashra was always the pariah that others whispered of, the child of the consort. They also called her the child of the void, for they believed that without any senses, she couldn't feel. 
But she was not deaf. She heard and understood what they said around her, what they said about her. Rather than bear the wounds their words inflicted, she pushed them aside and found solace in a place where only she could be, where there was nothing but silence. She called this place, her world, the Empyrean. Here, she was whole. She had eyes that could see and a voice that could speak. At first it was lonely, and she would cry inside, alone in her mind, on a small island that floated upon cold waters. But as she aged, a small sprout had taken root at the center of the island. Though it was always night in the Empyrean, shortly after, the first star appeared in the sky, bringing with it the gift of light.
She plucked this first star from the Empyrean, as easily as plucking fruit from a tree. She held it between her thumb and finger and put it up to her eye so that it was all she could see. Inside this star was another image. Of a maintenance hatch upon the wall of the Edict, and objects that she did not recognize by sight but would know if she touched them. They were held by hands and twisted and turned until a spark appeared and a panel lit up again. The two large hands, hands that belonged to a man, placed the panel back on the wall and wiped the sweat off his brow. She took the star away from her eye and put it back into its rightful place in the sky.
As she grew older, more stars filled the empty void above her. When she put each to her eye, the star replaced her irises and she saw the world as that person did. From the cooks in the galleys, to the commanders of the armies that would rest aboard the ship with their servants, to the captain and the crew that oversaw the weapons of the ship as they bombarded enemy territories from the safety of low orbit. She had learned much this way, but had no way to share it with anyone.
She was bursting with the desire to tell. One evening, she focused her thoughts on the young daughter of a mechanic. Ong was Ashra's age, and the girl would play with the other workers' children, in the sooty dark corners of the ship where adults didn't go. She wasn't too smart but she had a free spirit, and Ashra enjoyed watching Ong wander the ship, and in her mind she followed where Ong went. One day, Ong had found an old projector, a catalog of the different plants and animals of the Empire's worlds. She flipped through the holograms quickly, not really paying attention. But when Ashra saw a hologram of a plant that looked like the one that grew on her island in the Empyrean, and she had to know where it came from.
Ashra first asked with her voice, but it only echoed around the island and sea around her and did not leave the confines of her existence. So she asked with her mind, and when Ong did not respond, Ashra tried harder, finding her way into the girl's auditory cortex with the question, <What is it?> In a few days, Ong and her family were transferred to another ship because the child had gone insane, or so Ashra had overheard the medic say. She never learned the name of the plant that grew on her island.
She tried again, gentler, with her mother, and in time Ashra learned to speak with her mind. Lydia didn't know how to communicate that way, so she responded with her physical voice. Those that overheard the one-sided conversations thought Lydia had gone insane as well. They thought that she contracted some disease from her strange daughter, and the two were both avoided, yet respected, for they were still related to the emperor.
Ashra felt the guilt of having dragged her mother into shame, and the child wept because of it. But Lydia was already too enveloped in her own shame to notice, and she wept only tears of joy now that her daughter could tell her all that was in her heart.
Now at eight years old, the sprout had turned into a sapling. The island had grown as well, though Ashra could still see its edges as the water lapped at the sand around it. Large stars started to appear in the middle of the sky, motes of color that never moved and never disappeared. She called these stars the perpetuals.
She tried plucking these perpetuals from the sky, but not matter how she tried, she could never move them. When she stood on her tiptoes and brought her eyes close to them, she could never see anything past their bright hues. But she remembered the last time she saw her mother, and with her passing, the great magenta perpetual that had been at the very center of the Empyrean also disappeared. Ashra knew she would never see that color in the Empyrean again.
Another one of the perpetuals, a pale yellow star off to the side, was especially bright. Though these stars never changed positions and colors, she had never seen the yellow one this bright before. She reached out to touch it.

Chapter 10

Aki watched from the observation deck as Alan kept his hand on the girl's arm. The efficient, ruthless, and cynical man that she had come to respect as her teammate had never shown compassion or mercy on the battlefield. She kept a watchful eye on the graphs and charts that cycled across her light console, but an even closer eye on Alan.
After the child had not stirred after thirty seconds, Claudia grew impatient. "Send me a transcript and recording of what she says."
"What if she won't talk?" an engineer asked.
"Make her talk." Claudia turned and left the observation deck with the majority of her guards. The remaining ones spread out across the control room.
Not moments after her departure, a man with beady eyes and a red beard crept onto the testing facility. He tried to inherit Claudia's commanding presence surreptitiously, but his paunch interfered with how well he could keep silent as he moved across the hall. Aki noticed him first from the sounds of his labored breathing. His creeping had exhausted his energy and he was panting by the time he arrived at the observation deck.
"Simmons, what are you doing here?" Aki asked, her eyes never straying from her light console.
He took deep gulps of air before deigning to answer. "That", he gasped, "is none of your concern. You aren't even an official soldier of the Einhart. Just an Auroran assassin." He tried uttering the last word with contempt, but it came out as a wheeze. Aki counted two beats then hit the broadcast button as Simmons started his next sentence. "I am Fitzgerald Simmons, Second Medium of the Einhart! I shall not tolerate insubordination." His bark left him deflated.
"Since I'm just an Auroran assassin, you can tolerate it," Aki said and gave him the finger. Her other hand danced over the light console and selected the syringe. She maneuvered the needle into the child's skin.
The girl rested upon the medbed that had been hastily brought in. Her breath no longer turned to mist as warm air was filtered into the quarantine room. As the cocktail of vitamins and drugs entered her system, her eyelids quivered once then lifted, revealing two white orbs. Some engineers above gasped in shock, but it mattered little to the ones who had been through battle, those who had seen much worse than two blind eyes.
The windows of the observation deck became tinted and Simmons's voice came through the speaker. "We rescued your ship from the Edict, which has since been destroyed. You are currently safe with us, but that may change depending on how you respond to our questions." He let the threat hang in the air. "What is your name?" he asked.
The girl brought her hand to her throat and shook her head.
"She can't answer," Alan said.
"Don't tell me what it can and cannot do, Alan Belmont. For all we know it could be the very weapon that destroyed the Edict and is waiting to do the same to us."
Alan glared. "She can't answer if you ask the wrong questions," he retorted. He turned back to face Ashra and put his hand over hers. His tone changed and his voice became soft. "Do you understand me?" he asked her. "Nod if you do." He had forgotten what it was like to speak like this, in that voice adults reserved for talking to children and pets. It was gentler than he remembered it, though it came out rough from lack of use.
The girl nodded once.
"Good," he said. "We aren't here to harm you. But we found you pretty beat-up and you've been resting here since we rescued you. Is there a way you can communicate with us?" he asked.
She nodded again. Alan felt a tingling at the base of his skull, as if a drop of warm water had been placed there. The warmth spread up from his spine, into his brain, and seeped into his mind. He noticed Argus fidgeting behind him as the big man experienced the same sensations.
From that warmth came a breeze, as if he were back home on the empty trails of Cybele. On it rode a voice, a voice that sounded like it belonged to a girl older than the one in front of him. Like the rustling of leaves, words formed in his mind.
<I am Ashra, daughter of Lydia,> she said, though her mouth did not move and no sound left her lips. She cast her head about, as if she were looking for someone. <Was there another with me? My mother?>
Alan shook his head, but forgot that she couldn't see. "No," he said. "Only you were inside." 
She turned her head to the side. <Then I did not imagine it.> It started as a small tremor that bounced her shoulders up and down, followed by tears that formed at the edges of her eyes.
Simmons opened the com channel. "What did she say?"
"Her name is Ashra," Alan responded. "If you want to be a part of the conversation, get down here yourself or shut up," he said, knowing Simmons would remain where he was safe.
Alan turned back to Ashra and rubbed her back until the sobbing subsided into silent tears. She was strong.
<May I see you?> she asked. Her voice had regained some composure.
"You can see? How?" Alan asked.
<I need others to be my eyes, but I can see.>
Alan hesitated a moment, and then said, "Sure."
Ashra pressed two fingers to Alan's forehead, to the point where his eyebrows met. She pressed harder and the exertion creased the skin around her eyes and mouth, and he had to gently lower her hand to show her that it wasn't working.
"Maybe it's just me," he said. He waved for Argus to come over. "Would you?" he asked.
Argus lowered his gun and walked over. He had to kneel for the girl to reach him, but once she touched his face, he started to swoon and Alan had to use all his strength to keep him from falling. Argus sat on the medbed next to her with a hand on the edge to hold himself upright.
"I feel like someone else is here," he said drowsily, tapping his temple with a finger.
<Thank you,> she said. She saw the world through Argus's eyes. She saw the blacked-out observation deck above and the rugged soldier in strange clothes that stood in front of her. She saw herself, tiny and dirty. Her hair had turned completely white, and she ran her hand through it, feeling each strand slide across her fingers.
<It is rude of me to address only a part of the party if there are others that would like to listen. May I?>
Alan looked up. "She wants to speak to everyone on the other side of the glass."
"The guards and engineers will stay out of it, but the rest, including myself, will join," Simmons said. 
An engineer piped up. "Our sensors have detected a recognized waveform coming from the entity. The computer is processing it. It seems like the waveform can be decoded into sound waves."
"Do it," Simmons said. "Forward whatever you record to the High Adjudicant."
At once they felt the tingle in their minds. Simmons shook his head as if water were stuck in his ear.
<The Edict is no more,> she said. <If so, where am I?>
"You are safe," Alan responded, "aboard the-,"
"No more, Alan!" Simmons interrupted from above. "You've already shown her where she is; now it's time for her to answer our questions."
Even Alan had to agree he was getting soft.
Simmons continued. "When we rescued you, the Edict was being destroyed by a heretofore unseen weapon. What is your connection to this weapon?"
She paused. <Weapon? I don't know of any weapon.>
"Don't lie to me, child, if that's what you really are. A burst in delta waves was recorded as your escape pod left the ship. Your pod was the source of it."
<I still don't know of any weapon,> she responded. She sat up, dignity and confidence straightening her back.
"If that's how you're going to play it," he said, "we can always lock you up. There's ample space in our jails, and we have ways of getting you to talk. And if you don't cooperate, we can always use more biomass for fuel."
"That's enough," Argus said, looking up. "Regardless of where she came from, this is no way to get the information you seek."
Alan put a hand up, signaling for those in the observation deck to be silent. "Let's start with what you do know," he said. "Tell us what you remember."
Using Argus's vision to adjust her position, Ashra turned to face Alan. <I had not seen my mother in weeks,> she explained. <I wish I could've seen her more often, but they kept us separated, and I was always busy with my tutors. At a time when there were the fewest people awake aboard the Edict, she came for me and we fled.> She paused, trying to control her grief. 
Hearing her telepathic voice was an experience Alan had never felt before, and the same look of wonder and curiosity was on Argus's face as well. 
Ashra continued, <We were to leave together, to arrive here as mother and daughter, but in the escape bay, someone who was not one but not two?> Sorrow threatened to overtake her again.
Aki spoke from above. "Not one but two? What do you mean?"
Ashra turned in her medbed, looking straight at Aki through the opaque glass that separated her from the observation deck. <She moved as if she were one being, but the light inside was a mixture that did not mix, like oil and water. The two colors were not solid; she was two stars bound together with force. And there was a laugh, a laugh that celebrated remorse, one that celebrated death.>
Aki continued, "Her name? Was there anything-"
Simmons interrupted. "I ask the questions here, Miss Fushimi," he said.
Aki removed her finger from the broadcast button and pulled out a dagger. With a flick of her wrist, the edge of the dagger telescoped into a long blade. She pressed the blade against Simmons's throat, angling it under his beard and drawing a drop of blood. He tried to move his head away, but the feeling of cold metal against his neck suggested he stay where he was. 
Aki heard the ratchet of guns being cocked behind her from the guards that remained in the room, but she didn't care. Her face was as still and impassive as before. "I could kill all of you in this room and sustain at most one shoulder injury from him," she said. She nodded to one of the men that had his rifle trained on her. No one doubted her, not even the engineers that laughed at her earlier. "I won't kill you because I respect you. I ask for the same respect in return. Allow me to learn what I need to, as per my agreement with the High Adjudicant, and I will continue to assist the Einhart." She looked at Simmons, who quickly waved his hands below his waist. The men lowered their guns and Aki withdrew her blade. The metal retracted into itself and she returned the dagger back into the sheath strapped to her thigh.
"Ashra," Aki continued, "did you hear her name? What more do you remember about her?"
<Vijera. My mother called her Vijera, right before I left. And I tried, I tried so hard, but I couldn't peer into her mind; I could not see with Vijera's eyes.>
When Aki heard the information, her eyes narrowed into slits. Ashra described the woman she had been searching for, the reason Aurora had sent her here. "Thank you, Second Medium Simmons," she said. "Please continue with your questioning."
Simmons found his confidence again, though it wasn't as strong. "Ashra," he said. "If you know nothing about the weapon, which I don't believe for a second, then please explain your connection with the empire. What were you and your mother doing aboard the Edict? The empire isn't known for entertaining visitors."
She sat up straighter and held her head high. <Right before my mother's death, she said that I was to fulfill my birthright as a princess. I was going to be trained and eventually take my place as a royal.>
There was murmuring. A moment later, Simmons said over the com, "Don't be silly. A new child has not been heard of in decades and the ones that are already out there have merged with each other. We would've known of you."
Alan had also never heard of a royal child this young. With his doubt, he removed his hand from hers. "Is it true?" he asked.
<Yes. After my training, I will speak on behalf of the Sanctum Empire on the worlds that would like to join us. This was news to my mother, and for some reason she wouldn't allow it and tried to take me away. So I would like to know, where is this place? Is this where my mother intended me to be?>
Simmons spoke first. "Ashra, you are aboard a testing facility belonging to the Einhart. You are familiar with the Einhart, yes?"
<I have heard tell of you. When our soldiers go to battle they go against the Einhart, along with the systems that would seek your protection rather than join us.>
"Then you know you are an enemy and a threat here," Simmons said.
She nodded. <Too late, but yes, I now know,> she said. <But the empire has only sought harmony. I still do not understand why any world would choose to remain separate from the Sanctum. We distribute resources and the wealth of knowledge we have from the worlds in our domain. Most importantly, we offer peace.>
"Peace!" It was Alan who spoke. "Ashra, we, the Einhart, fight because the empire makes slaves of the people they conquer. Through fear. Through neural implants that force their soldiers to only think of the goals of the Sanctum. I have been fighting the empire for longer than I can remember; we all have," he said, gesturing around him, "and never once has the empire spoken of peace. They have only taken and conquered; and when they could not conquer, they destroyed."
Ashra was silent.
"If you are a princess, then you know much about the empire," Simmons said from above. "Regardless of if you're a child or a princess, you will tell us what you know. If you do not, we will pry it out of your brain. We have our ways."
He turned to the engineers, making sure to avoid looking at Aki. "Drug her. Take the escape pod to the engineering bay for further analysis. Find out what you can from it."
Ashra interrupted. <No,> she said, her voice firm. <I will go and I will cooperate, as a sign of goodwill between us. But I go on my own terms.> She pulled the needle out of her skin.
Simmons thought for a moment. "Very well," he said. "But you shall see what we think of the Sanctum. Even though we strive for peace, as you say your empire does, many families have been destroyed by your soldiers and they will show you their feelings." He turned to the guards. "Take her through the hub."
"Simmons," Alan said. "That's too much. She is still a child. The public would have no mercy in the hub," Alan said. But before he could continue his argument, the device on his wrist displayed a message above his palm.

She will be taken care of. 
-C

So Claudia had heard everything.
<As a sign of trust,> Ashra said, <I shall remove myself from the man who so graciously lent his eyesight.> She bowed once to Argus and pulled herself out of his mind. He shook his head to clear it then stood up, uncertain of what he should do next. He didn't want to hurt her; the girl was just a child. But he had his duty to uphold, his duty to Alan, not to mention she was the sister of the man who abducted the Erudite.
<I face my fate.> Ashra squirmed to the side and almost fell off the medbed, but Alan caught her before she could tumble to the ground. <Thank you,> she said to him privately. 
Though Alan helped Ashra up the stairs, she still tripped a couple of times on the steps. When they reached the top, a guard had already prepared a pair of handcuffs. He fastened them onto her wrists with no mercy, tightening the bindings more than he needed to. Men joined the Einhart for many reasons, but they all shared a common enemy. Ashra acted as if she didn't feel the pain.
Alan escorted Ashra down the hall. Argus, Aki, and the rest of the guards followed behind them. Their footsteps were in unison except for the princess's, who took shorter strides. They made their way down the extended tunnel that connected the testing facility with the rest of the Aurelius.
They stood in front of a bay of lifts. "If she knows the location of Sanctuary," Simmons said to Alan, "don't think for a minute I won't tear her limb from limb trying to find it. She is a threat and an asset, nothing more, and you had better remember it, Alan." He left.
Alan and the others boarded the lift that came after. It took them to the lower levels of the city that sat upon the back of the Aurelius. He had never ventured so far down, but he was glad with what he saw when they stopped. She would be kept in an isolated chamber with a few guards. One of them guided her to the center of the room, removed her cuffs, and stepped back. She rubbed her wrists as a hexagon of light lowered from the ceiling, securing her behind its six walls. It would be a lonely night for her, but no more lonely than her time aboard the Edict.
Alan looked back once then left without saying a word.

Chapter 11

"Guren, I need something stronger," Alan said.
"Alan, I can't keep giving these to you. Virifen aren't candy. You don't pop them into your mouth every time you feel the need to freshen your breath." But despite his words, Dr. Locus Guren had already reached underneath his desk and unlocked a drawer cleverly hidden away. 
They sat in front of a clear sphere. Behind that sphere, three monoliths, each an AI, towered over them and the rest of the room. Alan looked at them and considered the arbiters that made the decisions that affected the outcome of his last mission and many before it.
The AIs hung suspended in their own superconductive shell, cooled by the vacuum and darkspace sinks. Each one was an elongated rhombic prism filled with a near infinite amount of qubits for processing and maintaining the Aurelius. They controlled things from the levels of atmosphere in each section of the complex, the calibration and timing of the vacuum pipe transport system, to the repair schedule of the ships that needed maintenance. The work was spread over all three. They communicated closely, forming a black hexagon with their three sections, each section floating to its own rhythm. Banks of lights blinked on and off making them look like sleeping giants to Alan, though the multicolored electric currents that ran between them suggested that they were very much awake.
Dr. Guren was still searching under his desk when Alan asked, "Which one is Atropos?" Guren tried to stand up but bumped his head on the edge of his desk. Wincing, he rubbed his scalp and pointed to the one closest to them on the left. "That one. Why do you ask?"
"She seems to be the only one that likes me. The other two don't find me as attractive."
"Please, Alan. They are computers. Machines. To them, there's no such thing as like or dislike."
"But they're not just machines; they're AIs," Alan said, leaning back in the chair and putting his feet on the table that stretched around the sphere. When the Aurelius was in battle this table was filled with engineers, but right now, there were no light consoles and the room was dark.
"Programmed with human thought patterns," Guren said. "But they will not deviate from what they are programmed with."
"If so, then why does only Atropos agree with my actions?"
"Because like humans, they have each been programmed with different values, putting more weight on some issues and less on others. Clotho will always value life above anything else," he said, pointing to the one to the right. "While Lachesis is always hard to guess." He put a handful of Virifen pills on the table. "But a lot of processing happens in the background, while we only see their answers. It would take a lifetime to sort through the logic reasoning behind one decision."
Alan put his feet on the ground and looked at the pills. "And all three of them agreed not to give me anymore of these." He held up a green capsule between his fingers and studied it in the dim light. "I must be doing something right then." He put his pointer finger down onto the surface of the table and drew a circle around the remaining pills. An edge of metal rose from the table and surrounded the capsules, forming into a small container.
"Alan, Virifen alters the chemical composition of your brain. Considering your dosage and the time you've been taking them, I'm surprised you can still talk to me. Without the medbeds, your teeth would've been ground to nubs by now."
"You know I need these," Alan said.
"We are all here because of one kind of trauma or another. I'm not saying yours is any less; in fact, you've suffered more than anyone else I know. But there are healthier ways to cope."
"There'll be time to grieve when I'm dead. These numb the pain and give me the energy I need so I can focus on my job."
"But at what cost? They numb your humanity as well. Have you ever wondered what you'll do after the war is over? When there's no battle left for you to fight?" Guren's concern showed in the additional wrinkles that appeared on his forehead.
"You've been trapped in here too long, with these computers that argue with each other," Alan said. "When I'm out there, it doesn't look like the war is ever going to end. And I'm pretty sure I'll be dead before I see the end of it." Alan popped a pill into his mouth and bit down. "Despite the empire's actions, many worlds are afraid of us. Afraid that we'll take what little they have."
Guren shook his head. "They make their own decisions. We do what we can and you do what you can. But your body can't sustain this for much longer. Your mind should have fallen apart years ago. These were developed for temporary use, blocking emotions so soldiers could continue fighting the battle they were in. But it takes time to heal, time you haven't given yourself. You've been taking them ever since I've known you, ever since I joined the Einhart years ago."
"Maybe I'll allow myself the luxury of dreaming of the day when I won't need to get back into the Umbra again. We found something on our last mission, something that might make that dream come true."
"You mean the princess?" Guren offered.
"How did you know?"
"I monitor the AI, checking for any anomalies in their systems. And when all of their computing power is dedicated towards one event for a prolonged period of time, I am interested."
"That wasn't for you to see."
"But I saw it."
Alan smiled. "You're so tricky," he said. "She could be the key. She could know where Sanctuary is. We could finally take the war to their homeworld, invade them as they invaded us; show them the pain we have felt." He thought for a moment. "Speaking of which, is there a cure for her condition? It seems strange that a princess would remain blind and mute."
"Extending health benefits to our enemies now?" Guren chuckled. 
"Just trying to be compassionate. It's this new mindfad I'm trying," Alan said jokingly.
"I've looked at her genetic code and she didn't lose her eyes and voice to any disease or injury," Guren said. "A portion of the DAX genes are missing; it's as if her DNA never coded for those parts to begin with. Even if you put her into a medbed, it wouldn't be able to regenerate what wasn't there to begin with."
"Hmm, that doesn't seem like something the empire would allow?" He remained silent as he pondered the strange guest he had brought back.
Guren looked at Alan. "There's more to it, isn't there?" the doctor asked. "Why are you here? With your clearance as Second Medium, you could get those anywhere," he said, pointing to the pills on the table. "What else do you want?"
"I need a spare display, loaded with recordings of our battles with the Sanctum, including the most recent skirmish on Galea." 
"Those are classified. They have our complete battles, our strategies, our weapons, our troop arrangements. In the wrong hands, an enemy could predict our future deployment schemes and counter our stratagems."
"Give Claudia more credit," Alan said. "She hasn't ever used the same formation twice. And you can edit out some parts. I just need to show the empire's aggression. Their ruthlessness. The conduct of their soldiers and commanders. Though I know we don't have much, any footage of Seth and Vijera would be useful too."
"Releasing footage is something only the Sisters of Fate have permission to give," Guren said.
"Then ask them," Alan said.
Guren sent in the request to the three AIs. Moments later, three holographic displays activated on top of the table. Singular points of light expanded into three young women, triplets, wrapped in togas. The three looked the same: miniature, translucent and beautiful, except the colors of their hair were different. Two of them faced Alan, smiling, while one remained with her back turned, her blonde braid displayed in defiance.
"Truly, you are a favorite of Atropos," Guren said. "Lachesis has also taken your side, so you will have what you desire. Perhaps they see reason where I cannot." The Sisters winked out of existence. A holographic display cube, like the one Alan had in his room, emerged from the table. Alan took it and ran through some of the images, looking at battles that took place on different worlds. He was afraid he might've lingered on one planet, Cybele, but he flipped through it as quickly as he did the others. The pills did their job.
"Looks good," he said, putting it in one of his pockets. "Thanks for these," he said, shaking the small container of pills. He turned and started to walk away.
"You're going to show it to her, aren't you?" Guren asked.
Alan didn't stop walking.
"What do you see in her?" he called from behind. "She would be the same age, wouldn't she?" his words echoed in the empty chamber.
Alan stopped and turned. His face betrayed no emotion. "Don't ask questions you don't want to hear the answers to."

Chapter 12

Alan was greeted in the front of the isolation cell by a different guard from the one earlier. "Sir!" The man snapped a crisp salute when he entered. Alan nodded and the man relaxed.
He looked over the list of visitors, seeing no new names since he had left Ashra here. He identified himself with a press of his palm and the pair of doors opened with a hiss. The room was dark except the brightly-lit center, where Ashra sat encased behind the electric shield. The yellow shield crackled whenever dust came into contact with it, turning the motes to ash. She sat quietly with her eyes closed inside the hexagonal prison. Her dress had been replaced with another white dress, this time of Auroran cut. Despite being a prisoner, the Einhart tried to give her the respect that was befitting of a royal, regardless of the world she came from. She would want for nothing, other than freedom.
As his eyes adjusted to the dark, Alan made out another guard stationed in the corner of the small room, behind the enclosure. "I'd like to speak with her alone," he said.
"Yes sir." The guard jogged out of the room. Alan and Ashra were left in silence.
<You have come back,> Ashra said. <To what do I owe the honor of your visit?> Though her voice was young, it was humble and controlled, with no hint of malice or irony.
"I believe," Alan said, "that your education may have been incomplete."
<I was tutored by the best teachers of the empire in many fields, including diplomacy, mathematics, and science.>
He pulled up a chair and sat down in front of her. "But did they ever teach you of war?" he asked.
<No. The tutors always avoided the subject of the vastness of our domain, and I could never see the worlds the Edict visited. I was always limited to the people aboard the ship. And even now, I feel parts of this ship starting to close off, the stars in my sky growing fewer.>
It was true. After Alan had left, other Einhart had scavenged what they could from the husk of the Edict. Their scientists reverse engineered and replicated the technology to dampen Ashra's delta waves. They even attached some of the scavenged plating directly onto the Aurelius.
"They?we?are afraid of you," Alan said. "A girl that cannot see, but sees more than any one of us ever could."
<So my life has been and so it will be,> she said. Despite himself and the drugs that coursed through his body, Alan felt a pang of pity.
He was surprised that he was having this conversation with this child, whom he had only met a day ago. The girl should've meant nothing to him, but there was something about her that reminded him of a past he had tried to forget, but could never outrun. "The empire is not as kind as you believe it to be," he said.
<But nor is the Einhart. I am supposed to be somewhere else, yet the Einhart have abducted me midflight and now hold me prisoner. Yours is the only kind face I have seen, yet even you withdrew your hand when I said I was a Sanctum princess.> She did not need to pause for breath when she communicated telepathically, yet she hesitated, as if preparing herself. <Why?>
"Princess, you should know that your brother, your sister, your father?your whole damned family is responsible for the deaths of everyone I loved, back on my homeworld, Cybele. After joining the Einhart, my comrades and soldiers have been mowed down by your men, your biologics, and again by your family. That is why." He took out a pill and swallowed it, immediately feeling the emotion subside, replaced with only detachment and ice.
She chose her words carefully. <As someone who has known cruelty, I cannot condone violence and more cruelty as solutions. But as I said earlier, Mr. Belmont, I have difficulty believing what you say when I have not seen the destruction you speak of->
"And now you shall." Alan stood up, turned his back to Ashra and called the guard back in. The guard returned with quick strides and stood at attention.
"Your name?" Alan asked him.
"Tairus, sir."
"And your homeworld?"
"Adona, sir."
"The water belt?"
"Yes sir."
"And why are you here?"
"Adona was conquered by the Sanctum and it is my duty to avenge my homeworld and fallen worldmen. Sir!" He saluted.
"And what would you do to see the empire fall?"
"Anything, sir."
"Good." Alan gestured to the chair in front of the electric field. "Sit."
Tairus sat in one swift movement and remained unmoving, his hands in his lap. They really train these guards well, Alan thought.
"For the good of the Einhart and our cause, will you allow this girl inside your mind?" Alan asked.
"Yes sir!" There was no hesitation. This boy, for he was too trusting to be a man, was truly dedicated.
"Princess Ashra, try to see through his eyes as you did with Argus."
In the Empyrean, she saw the star closest to her, a dim one. She plucked it from the sky and held it to her eye. Her world changed and suddenly she saw herself sitting behind the barrier, staring at herself, with her white hair and white dress. Alan placed a small cube that floated in place between Tairus and Ashra. Its sides became translucent and light streamed out of them, coalescing into images. Images of destruction. She recognized the uniforms of the empire, their navy blue a stark contrast to the red of melting buildings, of fire, of blood. The things she had wanted to see all her life she finally saw; and she wished at once she did not see them. Faced with proof, the shield that protected her beliefs about her family began to crumble.
 "Tairus," Alan said, "I know it is difficult to see this. Adona is in this series. But keep your strength and remember your training in the academy. You'll be her eyes for the duration of the recording." He turned to Ashra. "Princess, it will be hard for you too. But look and listen to what your heart tells you is the truth. You will see what happened on Galea as well, when we took your escape pod into our custody."
Ashra was silent. She concentrated with her borrowed eyes on the scene in front of her, of Seth addressing a crowd of the recently conquered. Those that refused to kneel as the others did and those that stood up and shouted on behalf of their beliefs were quickly silenced by bursts of plasma.
Alan couldn't stand to be in the room a moment longer. He took the transport waiting for him up to the city where he sought a drink.

Chapter 13

Within the palace on Sanctuary, Seth, Voice of the Empire, sat in a chair floating above the ground. Freshly bathed and anointed with oil, he prepared for his journey on the road to conquest. He was at his desk, carved from an ancient arboreal that he felled after his second merge. He looked at a cluster of stars that floated in space before him, arranging motes of light in the shapes of his warships. 
Footsteps pattered from the hallway, the sound echoing around the tall chamber. A servant, tan and shirtless knelt before him.
"The Lady Vijera has returned. She awaits outside the chamber, First Prince."
Without taking his eyes off of the configuration of ships, he said, "Send her in." The servant hurried off.
He played with a few more arrangements then settled his hands and spoke into the darkness surrounding him.
"You can stop hiding, sister."
In one corner where the gloom was thickest, high above where the stone rafters held up the ceiling, a piece of darkness parted from the rest and slid forward, growing longer like a shadow in the dusk. The shadow took the form of woman and the Lady Vijera emerged, the darkness running off her suit like water. She kicked off the beam she stood on and flipped forward, past the windows and tall shelves of books. She landed gracefully on both feet. Only the sound of dust trailing her from above could be heard.
"I never could sneak up on you," she said. She came close to Seth and traced one finger across his broad shoulders, flicking one of his ears as she reached it. "Must be a gift from Prince Siris." She looked around the large library. "You could use better security, however."
Seth continued to look at his map of the stars. "I never needed security after merging with Siris. It was I that stepped out of the cocoon, and after him, there is no one left to be afraid of," he said.
"Except Father," she said.
"Except Father," he agreed.
"Now that he's cocooned, that puts you in charge of Sanctuary and the Sanctum Empire," she said.
"And I will make him proud," he responded.
Vijera smiled at the prospect of battle. "Which world shall fall to us this time?" she asked.
"Abyssinia. A land we already conquered," Seth said. "But they have become greedy, feeling that they deserve more than we give." He expanded the star map. A small planet appeared, its surface covered in gray and dark mountains. Everything was slick with water from the storm that roiled above. "There lies a nest of traitors here. They're selling technology off-world, technology we commissioned them to develop."
"Ungrateful scum," Vijera said. "What greater honor could there be than fulfilling the tasks we've given them to do?"
"Father saw value in keeping Abyssinia as it is. He said the information they exchanged with spies could help us as well, if we knew where to listen. Their spies were our spies, whether or not they wanted to be. But this affront has gone on long enough. Material gain rules their hearts and they must be punished."
"Has Father approved of your plans?"
"He will," Seth responded.
Vijera smirked at her brother's ambition. "He finishes merging faster every time," she said. "Will you be able to accomplish it before he emerges? Who will you send?"
"I will go myself. After Galea, I crave the taste of blood again."
"The Abyssinians are a valuable resource, brother. Can't we just use the neural implants like we do with all the soldiers?"
"The cost of invention is creativity and freedom of the mind. The implants dampen and suppress those qualities. So I shall administer virtue through fear and pain, things their minds will comprehend." He zoomed in on a valley between two mountains, onto a small square building. In the gloom, it would have been indistinguishable from the rest of the valley if not for the red lights on the roof. "Plans for our compact darkspace drive, the ones we had begun installing in select escape pods aboard Javelin-class warships including the Edict, were beamed off-world. I will go to make sure nothing gets transmitted ever again." He waved his hand and the maps collapsed into a beam of light and disappeared. 
He stood up and stretched. "It's a pity we lost the warship," Seth said, "but there are many to replace it. More importantly, what happened after I took the Erudite? What of the consort and Ashra, Princess of the Void? Did you retrieve her?"
A grin spread across Vijera's face. "She is dead," she half-sang. "The whore Lydia has perished by my hand." She pirouetted across the stone floor. "Now Father will be ours, ours again! No more sharing," she hummed.
Seth moved to the bookshelves that lined the wall, taller than even him. "And what of Ashra?" His finger stroked a volume bound in vellum.
She stopped dancing and looked aside, thinking of what she should say. Seth disappeared. A moment later the back of his hand struck her face. She collapsed and felt her cheek collide with the floor. A thin trail of blood flowed from a crack in her lips.
Seth's voice seemed far away. "Your silence is answer enough." He leaned over her, blocking out the light above. "Where is she now, Vijera?"
From the ground she held up a small black disk. Seth took the disk and threw it at his desk, where it was absorbed into the wood. A new display of stars was mapped out onto the air.
"You should thank me for the Auroran technology I brought to the empire, brother." Vijera spat the blood out and picked herself up. "I managed to tag her escape pod with one of the new tracers. Biologics that can withstand vacuum and transmit across distances never heard of. Here," she pointed to a bright blue speck hidden among the field of stars. "This is where Ashra's escape pod entered normal space. She can't have gone much further than that." She stood triumphant, sure of her absolution.
"Don't be so proud of yourself. It sickens me," Seth said. He rotated the field of stars, orienting the field with known clusters and nebulas. "You need Father's power to keep yourself in one piece. If it weren't for him, you, a former princess of the empire, would be the plaything of an Auroran assassin! You would be completely under Rin's control because she is stronger than you, Vijera." She turned away, not wanting to listen, but he continued. "And that is why you are no longer a princess, only a lady, because you were greedy. And you did it not for power, but for beauty, and this is what we are stuck with; a child that cannot control her own impulses. Do you know what they call you now? The Lady Gemini. Even the nobles mock us on Sanctuary!" He grabbed her roughly by the shoulder and turned her around to face him. She refused to look at him. "You know how hard it is to find a woman that can sire Father's children. You know! Most die while pregnant if they even make it that far. And you killed one of them."
