He walked to one of the edges of the island and felt the water lap at his toes. "I hate to leave you Ashra. I have a feeling I won't be able to come back, but I have to go. We're on an important mission." He took another step and turned around to face her, feeling the water tickle the edge of his pants. "As long as I know you're safe, I can continue without worry on my mind." He looked around, but didn't see any exit. "Uh, how do I get out?"
"I'm not sure, but I'm guessing you can leave the same way you came in. Just go to sleep."
"That may be hard-" he said, but when he thought of the act of sleeping he collapsed onto the sand as the Empyrean willed his thought into reality. The image of his body disappeared. All that remained was a yellow light that hovered in the air. Ashra walked over and put her hands around it. She gave it a lift and helped it float back into the sky. The pale yellow perpetual returned to its place beside the icy purple perpetual and another green one. They hung at the top of the Empyrean, unmoving and bright. "I always believed it was you," she said to herself.
Inside her pod, she continued sleeping.

Chapter 20

"Have you seen the new darkspace drive?" Aki asked.
"Yeah. The engineers don't waste any time," Alan said. He had woken up an hour ago from the dream he had of Ashra. Reality had returned, but the Empyrean clung to him tighter than any dream or nightmare he ever had. Out of instinct, he had reached for his bottle of Virifen pills. His hand had wrapped around the bottle, but some part of him said they weren't needed anymore, and that he would never need to take another. He left them by the bed.
They sat at the command console while Argus reconfigured the arrangement of missiles aboard the ship. "I'm surprised they allowed me to come," the big man said. "Isn't this supposed to be a stealth mission?" He pointed to himself. "As you can see, I'm not the stealthiest person." As if to give life to his comment, the chair groaned under his weight as he squirmed about, trying to find a comfortable position. The seat was designed for someone more Aki's size.
"You'll fit," Alan responded, "and if you don't, we'll make you fit. There's lots of room in space." He reconfigured Argus's chair from his light console. On his display, he pinched the edges of the base and pulled them out. In response, the nanomachines scrambled out of the floor and up the base the chair. The swarm surrounded the edges of Argus's seat and added material to where Alan had indicated.
Argus moved his butt around the widened base. "Thanks."
Alan nodded then opened the com channel. "This is the Umbra, hailing dropship Meridian." Judging by the distortion in eta-waves in the normally smooth planes of darkspace, an object moved parallel to them. Alan's transmission traveled through the new darkspace core before it was sent through an encrypted channel and received by the Meridian's own core. Despite being encrypted, the Einhart had always tried to keep their broadcasts to a minimum. The Sanctum had surprised them before with ambushes that the empire could've only known about if they had intercepted the Einhart's messages. Alan only sent the message when they were about to enter normal space.
"Hail Umbra. This is Captain Sylvan Winters of the Meridian. Your orders, Second Medium?"
"You are set to shift to normal space in T-minus 30 seconds. Are all personnel ready?"
"Aye, sir. We are prepared to reenter."
"Good. As discussed earlier, maintain a geosynchronous orbit with the dark side of the seventh moon after reentering. I'll test the bounds of this prototype drive and shift in as close as I can to Abyssinia. After scouting, we'll report back with details. Umbra out."
"May the Fates grant you a favorable wind," he said.
Aki watched the sensors as the eta-waves smoothed. "The dropship has left darkspace," she announced.
"We're flying solo now. Field report."
"Multiple targets detected in the surrounding space," Argus said. "Abyssinia doesn't have a planetary ring, does it?" 
"No, it only has a series of moons," Alan said. They floated through the field of particles, passing through them harmlessly in their own dimension.
"There's a spot on the edge that's barely wide enough for the ship." Argus marked it on the projection. The circled area appeared on their consoles.
"If we reenter normal space on top of something that's already there, what happens?" Aki asked. Her voice betrayed no hint of emotion, yet she gripped the edge of the command console, her knuckles white.
"The worst that could happen?" Alan said. "The atoms superimpose on each other, destabilizing their structures and creating a chain reaction that explosively decompresses the hull." As a friend, he enjoyed seeing Aki in those rare moments of fear. "Or?nothing at all. Shields up," he said. She activated them quickly.
With a flick of two fingers, he shifted the ship back into normal space. The bubble around them collapsed, sending out a small shockwave that rippled through the vacuum, pushing away silver objects that floated near the Umbra. 
Alan was impressed. Normal reentry was usually accompanied by a larger compression wave as the different spaces normalized. But this was like poking a hole through space that expanded to accommodate the ship, as opposed to earlier shifting, which was like tearing through the fabric of reality.
"Scan the objects," Alan said. "What are their compositions?"
"Mostly planetside metals," Argus said. "Some electric charge hasn't dissipated yet." He looked at a piece floating in front of the cockpit, its jagged edges exposing circuits and leaking plasma. "It looks like what's left of their orbital defenses." He watched it drift further away and collide with a small toroid. Alan had seen that shape before, released from the back of Seth's ship. 
The toroid mine detonated. The silent and bright explosion flung debris in every direction. Some of it was knocked towards Abyssinia below, causing the pieces to burn up in its atmosphere.
"Dropship Meridian, this is the Umbra," Alan said. Orbital defenses around Abyssinia have already been destroyed. Whoever did it left a surprise for us. The minefield makes a direct landing too risky and I'd prefer not to reveal our location by detonating the mines from our current position."
"Understood, Second Medium," Sylvan said. "My men are ready to clear the minefield."
"We don't have time the clearing squads need. We'll use comets and force detonation once we're through. Make your way here and come through the hole we create. Looking at the spread pattern, it may not be open long. Follow when you can. Dr. Ballard's transponder is active on the surface and we'll be aiming there. Umbra out." He checked his wrist-mounted map. The blue blip that represented Dr. Ballard shone brightly. "This is our rendezvous point," he said to his team, marking the point on their shared map of Abyssinia.
The three of them had suited up inside their comets. Each comet was shaped like a teardrop, designed for point insertion. The curved portion that was the base contained the equipment they would need on the surface. All along the sides, the comets were covered in plate defenses designed to withstand the heat and stress of atmospheric entry. Inside, the comets were snug and tight against their stretched-out bodies. Argus was so big that it would only close if he went in without his armor; he had to stow it underneath. Only their hands were free to manipulate controls mapped to each finger. They could crane their heads to look through the clear display that formed the top of the cone, but the comet was designed for durability, not mobility. 
They waited inside the launch tubes of the Umbra. In front of them was the field of debris interspersed with mines. "The Umbra will perform a short-range darkspace jump after we launch," Aki said.
Alan nodded. "Let's go." Three muffled thumps sounded one after the other across the Umbra's hull. Alan was shot into the debris field at breakneck speed. He would have preferred to have gone slower, but he didn't want him and his team to be easy targets for any possible ground troops below. His display showed his teammates through the tracking devices in their own comets.
But there was something else. Multiple objects trailed behind them. The mines weren't stationary, Alan realized. As soon as they sped past them, the mines raced after the comets, their burning cores making the center of each mine glow blue.
"Release beads when they're close," Alan said. Two lights winked in acknowledgement on his display.
Alan used his thumb and pinky for navigation. He guided his comet past the remnants of plasma cannon barrels. He dodged poles that were twice as big as the comet was long, support structures that once maintained the cannons. The mines were getting closer and proximity warnings sounded. He tapped his finger and a spray of tiny silver beads trailed out from behind him. The mines went after the beads and exploded behind the trio. The three flooded the space behind them with more beads. The beads dispersed throughout the wreckage and between the mines.
He continued navigating through the ruins of what once must have been a huge network of orbital defenses. As he dropped closer to the planet, he could make out patches of land between the turbid clouds. He caught glimpses of tall mountains and deep valleys covered in rain.
In a small gap between mines and wreckage, Alan checked the status of the ships. The Umbra had shifted to darkspace and was no longer above him. In its place was the Meridian, ready to descend. Everything was going according to plan.
In his moment of looking away, a mine had appeared in front of him. He was so close that it filled up most of his view. He tried tilting the comet, but it was too late to change directions. He braced himself against the pod, but at this distance the explosion would shred through the comet and his body. He narrowed his eyes, staring down whatever fate had in store for him.
"Boss!" Alan heard Argus shout right before the big man slammed into him from the right, jarring his head and the sensors. Instead of killing Alan, the mine detonated off to the side of Argus's comet and sent the Galean spiraling away. Smoke trailed behind him.
Alan cursed his own lack of attention. "Argus! Argus! Status!" He waited for a response, but there was only silence.
Shortly after, Alan made it through the debris. The outside of his comet started to glow orange as it entered the atmosphere. As much as his thoughts went to his teammate that just saved his life, the dropship still needed a way to get through. "Activate remaining beads," he said. Aki acknowledged.
Behind them, the beads detonated. The explosions set off a chain reaction that grew more violent as debris was thrown into the mines that surrounded them. Different flashes of color bloomed then quickly cooled in space. When the explosions stopped, a hole had appeared through the detritus. The Meridian began its descent.
Alan scanned the horizon for any sign of Argus's comet. He saw a small black spot with a trail of smoke behind it start to enter the atmosphere. Argus's comet wasn't spinning anymore; its bottom was facing towards the planet, which meant Argus was still alive to adjust its position.
"His course has stabilized," Aki said. "With his current trajectory he'll be a few kilometers away. We'll find him."
Alan activated his own acknowledgement light. He shifted the position of the comet with two presses of his fingers. His feet now faced towards the planet. True to its name, a trail of red streaked past the top of his head and behind him as he burned through the lower layers of the atmosphere. Landing thrusters activated, consuming the last of the fuel as they made minute changes in course and slowed his descent. When he moved through the dark clouds, lightning flashed around him and rain pelted his screen. He continued plunging past the bottom of the clouds. He could barely make out the ground below him, but magnification showed that whatever battle had taken place was already over. Bodies were strewn across the ground. He zoomed in; the wounds they had were bloody and ragged, nothing like the burn marks of plasma.
He braced himself for impact. Even with the thrusters on full it would be a rough landing. The display read three, two, one. He felt the ground beneath him shake. His knees bent and sank into the cushioned interior as the comet absorbed the shock. A moment after touching down, the front cover of the comet detached and fell away. Alan jumped onto the craggy rock that was the ground. Rain immediately soaked his hair. He held his pistol aimed in front of him. The air was full of the smell of ozone, made stronger every time lightning bolted across the stormy sky. He did a quick sweep of the area, his boots finding traction on the wet ground. When nothing came out of the darkness to greet him, he put his pistol away and watched as Aki landed. "Clear!" he called out to her. She nodded. 
After Aki left her comet, the upper part of the comets blew off. This left the comet bases, filled with their supplies, lodged in the ground. Alan grabbed his one duffel bag, hoisted it over his shoulder, and surveyed the area. 
Despite all the hills and mountains that dotted the distance, they were on a relatively flat piece of terrain. There was no dirt. Nothing grew here. The ground was made from hard volcanic rock, smooth from years of exposure to the elements. A square, squat building stuck out as the only manmade object on its surface. It took up little space; Alan could've jogged around it in a few minutes. He guessed that the rest of the research facility was underground.
 After he got out his equipment, he checked where Dr. Ballard was. The doctor's location hadn't changed since they had entered the atmosphere. When Alan walked over to where the blip was, he knew why.
Dr. Ballard was unrecognizable. Only his nametag, the blood washed off by the unending rain, identified the corpse. The man's face and clothes were torn to shreds. One of his hands, still clutching the offworld transmitter, lay a few feet away from the rest of the body. The blood was fresh, but it was too late to get any information from the doctor. Alan rummaged through his lab coat for anything useful. His hand closed around something hard and he pulled out a keycard. It was covered in gore but still in one piece. Alan thought the technology was obsolete, but it looked like it would still work.
"They still use these?" Alan asked.
"They never expect anyone to come here in the first place," Aki said. She was adjusting the sight on her sniper rifle, her favorite firearm. A sword sheath hung across her back, secured to her suit.
He pocketed the card. "It may come in handy," he said. He opened a com channel to the dropship. "This is Alan. We've arrived on the surface. What's your ETA?"
"Thanks for paving the road," Sylvan said. "The storm is making it difficult for our pilots. Barring electrical overload, we should be there in less than nine minutes."
"Understood," Alan said. "Aim for this position when you land. Secure the perimeter and find an entrance into the facility. We'll scout the surrounding areas while you're coming down. Alan out."
Aki slung her completed sniper rifle over her back. "Ready?"
Alan looked anxiously to where he saw Argus enter the planet. "Let's go find him," he said.

Chapter 21

Argus landed. It might've been a crash landing, considering how beat up his comet was, but he was alive. He turned his hand up and pulled the lever to release the front cover. Nothing happened. Maybe the mechanism was jammed, or perhaps the explosion warped the cover so much that it couldn't open. It didn't matter. His shoulder and back muscles were enough to crumple the cover. With a flex and a shove, it flew off and he stepped out into the rain. Cracking his neck a few times, he checked for any injuries. Other than a bruised side from the mine explosion, he seemed unscathed. The comet had taken most of the hit; mine shrapnel was embedded in the plating.

"In the distant morning rain
where the mountains end
and the clouds begin,
I do not know."

Though the poet Rudera was referring to the tranquil landscape of Galea and his ambiguous and growing feelings for his lover, Argus couldn't help but be reminded of the poem when he saw Abyssinia. But the rain was not distant; it coated him without mercy. There wasn't much to see other than the darkness, illuminated by stray flashes of lightning. Mountains surrounded him. A multitude of paths carved their way between them, though most led nowhere. He took the top of the comet and tried to remove it by pulling the latch on the side. When that failed, he grabbed it by the edges and flexed his hamstrings. He grunted as he lifted the bottom of the comet. In one movement he pulled it above his head and then threw it into the ground, smashing it into bits. His gear bag spilled out onto the wet rocks. 
He began to walk forward to retrieve his bag, but a quiet growling from behind stopped him. Slowly, he turned around. Under the shadow of a tall rock, two eyes leered at him. They were narrowed to slits and glowed red in the darkness. A third eye opened between the two. Together they looked like three rubies in the rain. Only when lightning illuminated the sky did Argus see the rest of its body. A long tail, segmented, thick, and muscular, waved sinuously behind it. It stood on four feet like a canine. Each foot ended in four claws that looked like they could rend through steel, flesh, and bone with one swipe. The head was long and topped with two pointed ears. Its wide maw seemed disproportionately large; saliva dripped from its lips and three rows of teeth gleamed behind them. It was covered in a layer of matted fur, but underneath it looked like it had the scales of a reptile. Argus was intrigued by its origins, but right now it looked hungry and Argus had a lot of meat. Study could come later.
Argus was crouched and his bag of weapons was still a few feet away. He could reach for it, but how fast was this creature? Only its tail and the three blinking eyes atop its head showed any movement. His pistol had been destroyed when the mine exploded, leaving him unarmed. Better to risk it while he still had the advantage of distance.
He dove for the bag and stretched his arm out. His fingers brushed against the handle, but the creature's tail knocked the bag aside before he could grab onto it. If the tail was already there-
Without looking, he flipped over, pulled back his hand and punched. His fist connected with the beast's muzzle, knocking it, along with a few teeth, away as it pounced for Argus's neck. It spun and landed on all fours, its growl louder now. The teeth grew back instantly, pushing out at sharp angles and cutting its own gums, causing blood to flow out of its mouth. No natural animal would do that to itself.
There was no time for Argus to get up before it jumped again. From his position on the ground, Argus kicked up, hoping to knock it back, but it whipped its tail on the ground and changed course in midair and landed on top of him, knocking the wind out of his lungs. Argus wrestled the beast on top of him, but it was strong. He fended off one set of claws only to be raked across his left side. Three long gashes appeared across his shoulder. He held it away from him with both hands clasped around its neck, trying to strangle it with his bare hands. In retaliation, its tail came and wrapped around his own throat. Out of instinct, he held his head down. He ignored the crushing of his windpipe and the slime that dripped over his cheeks as he sought to choke the life out of the beast. 
His face started turning blue when the beast suddenly fell limp, its brain splattered across the ground and onto the side of his bag. It was only a moment later that he heard the gunshot echo throughout the valley.
On a nearby rock, Aki whispered to herself, "Boom. Headshot."
Alan ran down the trail, his feet slipping on the smooth stone. As he rounded the bend he caught sight of Argus, throwing the carcass of some animal off himself. Alan came up next to him and put his arm under his, helping him up.
"What took you so long?" Argus coughed as he took in large gulps of air.
"Got into a bit of an accident. Some crazy guy slammed into me," Alan said. He could barely suppress his laugh. He embraced the large man, his arms not quite making it all the way around. "Thank you," he said into Argus's chest. Alan felt warm arms embrace him in return. "My duty," Argus responded.
They let each other go. Argus sorted out the last of his items from the comet, attached malleable armor across his body and patched up the wounds on his shoulder. Alan fished around in his pockets and threw him a spare communicator.
A voice came from behind them. "That's why metal is better than light." Aki walked up to them, her sniper rifle slung behind her. "With a light rifle, the rain would've absorbed the heat and this," she said, poking the beast with her boot, "would still have most of its head." She knelt down and ran her hands over it, studying its anatomy. She opened its mouth. A slimy tongue, swollen and purple, lolled out.
"Doesn't look indigenous to this planet," she said. "Biologic?"
"Seems like it," Alan said. "It's a little too well-built for anything other than killing."
"If they've set loose biologics across the planet, then they're not too interested in the people here anymore," Aki responded.
"Biologics?" Argus asked. He flexed his shoulder. It hurt, but he would live.
"The empire employs more than just people and ships," Alan said. "They didn't use them on Galea because they needed your Erudite. But when they're through with a planet, they let all sorts of engineered creatures loose. Think of them as the cleanup crew. Biologics will destroy remaining pockets of resistance while the empire moves on. Haven't seen one like this before, but there's always something new."
"At least we know we can kill it," Aki said.
The com line opened. "Second Medium, we've landed and are surrounded by hostiles!" It was Sylvan, speaking in between bursts of gunfire. "Second Medium, do you copy?" Alan heard shouts in the background followed by vicious growls. The gunfire was unfocused and random.
The three ran back up the path.

Chapter 22

If Lydia had been there to see it from orbit, she would've thought that Phalos was unchanged. With the exception of the patches of ground that had turned to glass from the brief war with the empire, the trees, rivers, and other agents of nature had reclaimed the rest without hesitation. Only its wide oceans stopped the progress of land, and within those blue depths laid mysteries unknown to both the people of Phalos and the empire. Sanctum still controlled it in name, but the forests were vast and the trails were narrow, and most had forgotten this planet. But Lydia was not there to see it, and Ashra remained in stasis as her pod began its descent into the atmosphere.
She arrived during the day when the sun was highest, and so her pod went unnoticed by most. But there were two who saw it as it burst through the mesosphere. 
The first was a Sanctum radar technician who sat dozing behind his console. The console beeped at him, trying to get his attention. He lazily opened one eye and logged the anomaly with a flick of his finger. There was always something coming in, whether it was a stray meteorite or some ship debris from the battle years ago, only finding its way down now as the gravity of Phalos pulled it in. This object was slightly larger than usual, but nothing outside of normal parameters. He would file it with the daily report to the Marquis after he was done thinking about the girl he left back on his homeworld. On Phalos, the days were long and the nights were even longer.
The other being that saw the pod stood crouched atop of tallest branch of the tallest tree in the forest west of the Marquis's estate. He was called Zava. He was small and lithe, weaving in between leaves like the wind. He was like any other who kept watch over the land, vigilant and unseen, camouflaged by the forest. The only thing that gave him away was his eyes, white and luminescent, as if they were powered by the sun itself. He and the other watchers would play games to pass the time, like trying to find a star hidden in the midday sky. Once they found it, they would turn their heads and stare at each other, count to ten, then whip their heads back up, seeing who could find the star again first.
They had spent the past month searching the sky for stars and something that should've been easier to see. It was Zava who spotted it first. He saw the orange and purple streak across the sky, coming from an object that was too round to be anything other than what they had been waiting for. It crashed in the fields to the west of the Sanctum outpost.
He clambered down the tree, his brown skin blending in with the bark as he made his way toward the ground. His movements were swift and the placement of his limbs precise. His feet dug into the notches in the bark, the green scales on his ankles and calves worn smooth from experience. His fingers, which had long since turned into claws, wrapped around the branches and trunk. He dropped the last few feet into the underbrush and landed on soft dirt and dead leaves. He ran deeper into the forest. There was scurrying as others followed him, eager to report what they had also seen. They were afraid to approach the maiden during the day, for she was tired and often lost control of herself in the bright hours, but this was something she would gladly awaken for.
They reached the mouth of the cave, where the darkness consumed what little light made it past the entrance. They stepped inside, their pace slow and their eyes cautious. The air was stale and old. Their footsteps left faint imprints in the dust behind them. Soon, all the natural light had disappeared and they relied on their glowing white eyes to see. As they progressed further into the cave, they went through so many spider webs that they didn't bother to peel them off their skin. Zava was in the front. He now looked more white than brown, but still they plunged deeper as his eyes led the way. The cave split into a maze of smaller tunnels and passageways, many of which contained the desiccated bodies of Sanctum soldiers. These soldiers were sent to their deaths when they tried to flush out the remaining inhabitants of Phalos and failed. Their corpses had all but turned to dust, and in some areas only the empty navy blue armor was proof that they had ever existed.
The group knew they had reached her chamber when the webs were thickest. All of the children had to pull and tear with their claws in order to get through. The walls were studded with moonstone, so called because they cast an eerie glow throughout the chamber like the two moons that circled Phalos. The light bathed everything in its softness. Jagged edges became round and smooth. The unending darkness gave way to the white light of the stones, content to share the space as they illuminated the woman in the center. More webbing surrounded her and nothing could be seen past their threads below her nude waist. Her hair was straight and black, only visible by the sheen it gave off in the dim light. Her arms were slender and her face was delicate, shockingly white against the dark hair that framed it. She was still and quiet. But a low hum and the skittering of legs echoed around her.
She raised her head and opened her eyes. There was a group of children standing in front of her. Their bodies had changed, and she felt a moment of regret for who they used to be, who they might've become, and who they were now. But it was only a moment, because the smiles on their faces showed that they didn't mind anymore. Perhaps it was only she that still grieved for something everyone else had forgotten.
Zava knelt on the floor, his knees becoming entangled in some of her webs. "Maiden Orana," he said, "the star has fallen! The star you mentioned. It's really come!"
With these words, the burden of her heart lifted and her weeks of waiting and prayer were answered. "Are you sure?" she asked. "What did it look like?" Her voice was soft and clear even after days of silence.
"It was big!" Zava demonstrated by throwing his arms out. "Bigger than anything I've seen fall out of the sky!" The other children murmured in agreement.
"Was it as big as the chunk that fell a year ago?" she asked. The hull of a Sanctum warship had finally succumbed to Phalos's gravity.
"Uh huh! It was!" He stretched his hands out so far that it looked like he might pull himself apart.
Orana reached forward and rubbed Zava's head affectionately with delicate fingers tipped with shiny nails. She took his clawed hands into her own. "You saw where it landed?" she asked.
He nodded. "Over there." He pointed to the west. "Far from the people." By people, he meant the Sanctum. They, Orana and the children, were no longer people. 
"Gather the fastest runners and be swift. Find it before the empire does. I'll be there to protect you."
At this the children cheered, waving their clawed hands in joy. The maiden rarely came out of her cave and it was a special event to have her join them. They bolted from the cavern. Only Zava was left behind, stuck to the ground by the webs he knelt in. Orana bent down cleared them away with a sweep of her hand. He bowed once then hurried to catch up to the rest.
Now that the children had roused her, she awakened herself fully. Her irises became encircled by rings of red. Six crimson eyes glowed underneath the webbing. She rose upwards as if an ocean wave carried her forward, but there was no water beneath her. The head of a spider emerged as the webbing fell away from her complete form. Her upper body grew out of the spider's head, beautiful and radiant, but from her waist down, where two equally beautiful legs should've been, was an enormous spider covered in short black hairs. Two fangs curved in the dim light. Poison dripped from their tips and sizzled on the stone below. Eight muscular legs broke free from the webs that had hid them, their span reaching from wall to wall. She pressed forward and the remaining white filaments fell away from her like a silken cloak, revealing an oval abdomen that left a trail of fibers behind it. Atop it was a crest emblazoned in white; three tear-shaped orbs with long tails that wound into a circle. Some say it looked that way because she cried for everyone Phalos had lost. She herself thought it looked like cosmic dust being sucked into a black hole. But when the Sanctum saw the emblem, they knew only death.

Chapter 23

Aki took position on a rocky outcropping as Alan and Argus ran ahead. She brushed a strand of wet hair behind her ear and adjusted the sniper rifle on the rock. The bipod was made of two tendrils of electricity, similar to the tethering that bound the engines to the Umbra. They reacted to the motion of her arms, changing height as she moved the rifle up and down from her high vantage point. She pressed her shoulder into the rifle stock and fixed her eye to the scope. When she looked through the scope, the rain disappeared as an infrared filter covered her view. Heat showed up in various shades of red and yellow. She took aim at the nearest orange object galloping on four legs. She exhaled, steadied her body, and pulled the trigger. There was a loud report. Red and orange splotches appeared on the ground, quickly turning green and blue as they cooled in the rain.
She knew the rhythm. Grab the knob. Pull up, pull back, push in. A round slid into the chamber. A new target, this one with its jaws around a soldier's wrist, dragging him away. He was screaming. His head blocked her shot, but she was patient. Waiting. Waiting for the right moment. It would come. The beast reared up, ready to eviscerate the man with its claws. Exhale, pull the trigger, report. One shot, one kill. The soldier wouldn't be able to use his hand, but he would live.
Death was so impersonal this way, but she didn't mind. Death was her job, not something to like or dislike. She did her job with accuracy and efficiency. She felled another two of the beasts before Alan and Argus appeared at the edges of her scope.
Alan held his own smaller rifle at the ready, unloading three-shot bursts as he entered the fray. Two of the beasts immediately turned on him. His bullets slowed one of them as they impacted into its chest, but the creature continued forward, oblivious to the pain. It wasn't until he fired into its third eye in the center of its head that its four legs crumpled and it fell. The other leapt at him from a boulder, its claws flashing as lightning illuminated it in midair. Alan aimed his gun up and pumped bullets into its exposed chest. When it didn't flinch, he threw himself to the ground as it swiped at where his head was moments ago. He fired three more shots from his supine position. The beast landed on its side, collapsing onto the hard rock. Blood pooled around it. Yet its tail still whipped around, threatening to choke whatever was close. Alan put another round into its head. The creature was still.
Alan caught a glance of Sylvan Winters, a rough and rugged man with a graying beard, sitting on the ground. The man clutched a gash that ran across his forehead. The blood seeped into his eyes and he couldn't see, which meant he couldn't give orders. Alan knew that at this rate everyone would be dead before they made it inside the facility.
Alan accessed the platoon-wide com. "What's the status on the door?"
"Can't get it open, sir! Code ain't workin'!" A soldier responded between bursts of gunfire.
Alan saw a small soldier, the technologist, with a device hooked up to the door. Another soldier guarded him. "Gather at this location," Alan said, marking the entrance to the building. "We'll hold them off. Find a way inside!"
The scattered troops started backing up, pulling into a tighter formation. The ones that had spare hands grabbed the wounded and dragged them towards the rendezvous point. Alan was glad to see Sylvan stumbling along with them. He would need a commander once they were inside.
Alan fired two bursts at the nearest beast. Another one jumped from the top of the dropship; Alan spent the last of his ammo taking it down. He was almost at the door when another one leapt from the roof of the facility. Putting aside his empty rifle, he grabbed his knife from its sheath. He braced for its attack, bending his knees and holding the blade out, hoping to slice its abdomen before it landed. But the claws never came. It was blown backwards onto the roof it leapt from as a sniper round ripped through its head and out its back. He could barely make Aki out against the rock she laid on, but he nodded his thanks. He was sure she saw it.
He ran up to the technologist. "What's the problem, soldier?" Alan asked. He saw the name Samson stitched onto his uniform. Samson had a boxy device attached to the entry pad. It scrolled through different combinations of letters and numbers and symbols Alan had never seen before, but it couldn't crack a single character of the entry code. Alan pulled out the keycard he had taken from Dr. Ballard and waved it front of the pad. There was recognition and a welcome sign, but a moment later it asked for fingerprints. 
Alan sighed. He scanned the battlefield for the doctor's hand. To his surprise, it lay where he last saw it, the communicator still gripped in rigor mortis. It seemed like the creatures didn't go for things that were already dead. Alan charged forward, feet slipping on the wet stone. He dodged two of the beasts as he made his way towards Dr. Ballard. He dashed past the body, slid the last few feet and grabbed the hand. When he turned around, he was faced with a set of jaws. He pulled his head away right before the teeth clamped down. Tendrils of saliva coated his face as he shuffled back.
A roar, a human one, was the only thing he heard before Argus came crashing forward and rammed the animal with his shoulder. The two barreled past him.
"We have guns for a reason!" Alan called to Argus as he got up.
"No fun!" he yelled back. "And we need to conserve ammo!" he said between punches.
Alan went to the building and threw Dr. Ballard's hand at Samson. The technologist fumbled with the dead man's hand, disgusted with the appendage.
"Scan it and open the door or we're all dead," Alan said.
Samson's head bobbed up and down. He proceeded to pry open the fingers one by one.
Alan radioed Aki. "I need you here."
"Been a long time since a man said that to me," she responded.
Back on the rock, Aki slung her sniper rifle over her back. The creatures had noticed where they were taking the heaviest fire from. Two of them rushed towards the rock she was perched on. She had sacrificed stealth for speed, and after the first round of bullets, it was easy to see where she was shooting from. They were getting closer, but she waited. Timing was everything. The first one leapt, its hindquarters propelling it high. She ran and jumped forward. As she pushed off, she pulled her sword out from its scabbard on her back. The silver blade of the katana flashed once as she pulled it through the air and the rain. The beast's head fell to the ground. The rest of the body continued sailing ahead. It crashed lifelessly onto the rock she left. She twisted her body and somersaulted through the air. Another one was below her. She gripped the handle of the sword with both hands and drove the blade deep. It entered from the top of its back, through its heart, then out its chest. It was still when Aki's feet hit the ground. She removed the sword and flicked the blood into the rain with one swift movement. When she sheathed the blade, the beast fell over.
With hydraulic clicks, the doors finally opened into the wall. Alan grabbed a fresh magazine and kept the animals at bay while the soldiers dragged the wounded inside. He looked for Aki in between shots but couldn't find her through the rain. He scanned the trail from where they had come and caught a glimpse of black on black that darted between the rocks. Only her face, white as moonlight, gave her away in the darkness. Behind her was a train of at least five of the creatures, maybe more. He aimed but couldn't take a shot. She ran straight for him and blocked his line of sight.
His com crackled. "Close the door," Aki said.
"I'm not leaving you out there," he replied. "Hurry up."
"I'll make it," she said. 
Alan trusted her. He looked back at Samson. "Close the door!"
"Sir, she's still outside!" he protested.
"Do it." Alan's eyes made it clear it was an order.
The doors started sliding in from the wall, rumbling along their tracks as they converged towards the middle of the opening. Aki ran, but the beasts were faster and were about to overtake her. When she was a few steps away from the door, she pivoted on her foot, faced the creatures and leapt backwards. In midair, she reached to her sides for two pistols. With one in each hand she unloaded four rounds at the ones closest to her. They collapsed and the others tripped over their bodies. Still gripping the guns, she arched her back and landed on her hands. Flexing her shoulders, she pushed off the wet ground. With a final backflip, she made it inside the facility. The doors closed as her fingers slipped past. They sealed shut with a thud and the sound of rain was silenced. Low growls and scratching could be heard on the other side, but they were safe for now.
Aki holstered her pistols and walked towards the back of the room as all the soldiers looked at her. Some looked in fear, some in shock, all were in awe.
"Where did you find her?" one of them whispered to Alan.
"Aurora." 
The soldiers nodded knowingly.
Alan at last had a chance to look at the building he was in. It was a wide space. The lights hung in rows from the ceiling and illuminated the concrete floor. He put his hands to the walls and tapped. Solid and reinforced. Nothing else would be able to get in, he hoped. There were a few doors on the far end, but other than that, the room was empty. That bothered him. The one entrance to a research facility should've had more foot traffic and more security, but there was nothing here now. Whoever had unleashed those things outside had already made their way down.
As Alan looked around, Sylvan approached him. A bandage had been wrapped around his head. The majority of the bleeding had stopped, but there was still a gash that ran from his forehead and down his cheek. Sylvan looked at the ground as he spoke. "Thank you, Second Medium. It was my job to command and I wasn't able to. If it weren't for your team, we would still be outside, torn to shreds."
Alan put a hand on his shoulder. "There's no need for thanks. We all make mistakes." Alan nudged him with his palm and they moved away from the soldiers that could overhear. "You're lucky you have a chance to fix it," he said. "My team and I have our own orders from the High Adjudicant, and I'll need a captain to lead the others. Can you do it?" Normally Alan would've upbraided the captain for being incompetent, or worse, he would've ignored him completely. He didn't know why, but he felt like forgiving the man.
"Sir, yes sir!" Sylvan's hand quivered from the adrenaline, but his voice was firm.
"Good," Alan said. "First, treat the wounded. Our contact, Dr. Ballard, the one you were supposed to befriend, is lying in pieces outside. We'll need to get our information by other means. Secure this area; I don't want any of those things coming in from the ceiling or the shadows. When you're done, find a way into the main structure."
"Understood, sir. What were those biologics anyway?" Sylvan asked.
Apparently they hadn't gone far enough. A soldier overheard them and put down the skin sealant he was applying to his arm. "Hey Deacom, what'd you call 'em?" he called to another soldier squatting next to him.
"Fren."
"Like Captain Winters's ex? Frennen?"
"Yeah, 'cept just half her name cause they're only half as vicious." The room filled with laughter. Alan was glad that the troops still had their morale. The captain scowled at the mention of his past.
Sylvan strutted back and barked out orders. "I want everyone patched up and ready to go in five minutes. Give me a tally of the wounded and killed. You all who can walk, grab your weapons and secure the area. Find a way down. We're going to finish our mission and see what we can find."
After four minutes, Alan looked at those that remained. They had lost five; their bodies were still outside and left to the fren. There were ten members of the platoon remaining, eight that could fight. The two that couldn't hold weapons, including the one Aki had saved, would be surveillance and intelligence. Though they couldn't shoot, they would still assist their team.
The group headed towards the far wall. There seemed to be an elevator of some sort, but it required another handprint and Samson had left the hand outside. Alan decided not to use it. He didn't know what surprises this place had in store, and he didn't want to bring up an elevator car full of them. 
There was a smaller door next to it. There wasn't any handle, and a quick push showed that it was locked tight.
"Argus!" he called.
The big man came up and put his bag on the floor. He opened it and pulled out two shaped charges made from sticky plastic with imbedded micro-triggers. He was always fond of explosives, and he was glad to finally have a chance to use them outside of his training on Galea.
He looked for joints on the door, and finding none, turned the putty into long strips. With forceful pushing, he inserted the strips into the seams where the door met the frame. There wasn't any cover, so everyone ran back as far as they could.
Argus stepped back and looked at the readout displayed in front of him, his finger ready. "Detonating on three. Five, four, three-"
An explosion rang out and the door disappeared, clanging as it fell down a long staircase. They kept their guns trained on the darkness. When the smoke cleared and nothing appeared, they marched forward, their steps steady and their gun sights level.
Sylvan tagged four soldiers. "Squad A, you're with me at the vanguard. Deacom, take the rest. You and squad B will bring up the rear."
"Sir!" They gathered into formation, clustered around the two weakened soldiers and Alan's team. Despite Alan being military, it was an unwritten rule that a Second Medium was to be protected at all cost. With only their gun lights illuminating the darkness, they descended.

Chapter 24

They went down the stairs quickly; their footfalls on the metal steps sounded like the rain outside. After six flights they saw light at the bottom. The door was already open. After a quick sweep, they stepped out. Sylvan waved for his team to disperse and check the area for survivors or enemies. Alan entered behind them and saw the true entrance to the facility, deep underground.
A statue of a woman stood at the center of a wide atrium. It was carved out of a hunk of black rock, the same rock Alan had slipped across earlier on Abyssinia's surface. Golden wings unfurled from the sides of its head, pulling its face and body up in a gesture of hope, or so Alan thought. "The burden of science is on our shoulders," was inscribed along its pedestal. Next to it, with his arm around the statue's feet, hung the limp body of a scientist, his lab coat smeared with blood. Alan checked his pulse and felt nothing. The wounds that went through his chest were different than the ones caused by the fren. These were precise and clean. Three round holes entered his front and exited his back. 
"Have you seen anything like this?" he asked. Everyone shook their heads.
They moved forward past the statue and the empty reception desk. There were no more bodies in the area, but there was blood on the floor. The blood streaked across the tile and continued onto the walls. A congealed substance coated the path the blood took, as if a hunter dragged its prey away to consume it elsewhere. Samson put his hand to it and felt the substance with a couple fingers. When he tried to withdraw, he had trouble pulling his hand free and an even harder time untangling his fingers from the clear slime. He wiped his pants with a grimace.
"Keep an eye on the ceiling," Alan said.
Behind the reception desk, the hallway split into two corridors. The walls were the same in each corridor and no sign indicated where either went. Doors lined both halls; the rooms they led to were all dark.
"Should we split up?" Deacom asked. "We could cover more ground faster." 
"No," Sylvan said. "The fren took out a third of the platoon, and we don't know what else is here." Even he took to using the name of his ex for the beasts.
"Which way should we go then, sir?" Samson asked.
"You're the technologist, shouldn't you have the map?" Sylvan growled.
"Sir, I haven't been able to find an active console. Without one, I can't download a map."
Neither of the paths was labeled; there was no indication of what each one led to. Though Alan didn't want her here in this dangerous place, he wished he had Ashra's gift of sight beyond sight; he wished that he could see past the walls and the rooms and see if there was anyone who could tell them what had happened here.
When Alan did not offer an opinion, Sylvan took the lead. "We go right." He marched confidently down the corridor and the rest followed. They continued deeper into the facility, ever wary for anything that could surprise them. They shone their lights into the rooms, but when all they encountered was lab equipment and conference tables, they passed the rest of the doors quickly and quietly. All Alan heard was the jangling of their equipment and the nervous breathing of the men.
They continued further into the facility and the air cooled as they walked down a slight decline. The corridor ended in a set of heavy doors that were already open, the entrance obscured by a veil of mist. They stayed in formation as they entered.
Their footsteps sounded hollow against the metal grating. Alan's foot bumped into something hard. He waved the mist in front of him away. A large glass tube appeared, filled with some kind of clear liquid. It looked like a test tube, as if something should've been inside of it in suspended animation, but he saw nothing. Thick wires connected it to the ceiling and Alan saw more wires run underneath the grating and into the floor. Whatever grew in the tube was big, and Alan was glad it wasn't inside the tube anymore.
"What kind of research did they do here?" a soldier asked. He was peering into another tube.
"We purchased light-based weapon and pre-fabricated darkspace engine schematics from them," Sylvan said. "All of that stuff is mechanical."
"Huh. This looks kinda biologic to me," the soldier said, tapping on the glass.
"This is Sanctum territory. They did a lot of things we didn't know about-"
"Shh." Aki put a finger to her lips. The hairs on the back of her neck stood up and she knew to trust her instincts. Something was wrong. As she thought it, floodlights turned on from the ceiling, casting the room in a harsh glow. The soldiers could barely see each other through the low mist that covered the lower level, but the second level that revealed itself in the light was clear. A walkway wrapped around the wall of the upper floor, and in its center was an observation deck. Behind the glass of the deck was a fren. It sat stiffly, its mouth dripping with saliva and its three eyes fixed ahead. Stroking its head was Vijera, the Lady Gemini.
Upon seeing her, Aki put a hand on the grip of her blade.
Vijera spoke through the com system and her voice echoed around them. "So glad you could make it," she said. "I was getting bored here all by myself." She kissed the fren on the muzzle and nuzzled it with her nose. "The pets helped, yes they did, but no one put up a fight. No fun at all. Which is surprising, since they made weapons here. What good are they if no one knows how to use them?" She laughed. Her voice was soft and high, touched with the instability of two souls wrestling for control over one body. Aki heard her sister's voice somewhere in there and refused to acknowledge it. This was Vijera. Her sister, Rin, was gone.
When she regained control of herself, Vijera said, "This biologic research was funded by the empire, and I think it's time to see what they did with our money." Her hands moved around on the console. "I hope you live, I really do. It's been so long since I've had a challenge." She looked Aki in the eye and blew a kiss at her. A second later she disappeared out of the back of the observation deck. Aki was prepared to climb atop one of the big tubes and launch herself onto the railing on the upper level, but at that moment, the glass tubes exploded. Those nearest to the tubes were splashed with the liquid inside. They gagged. It wasn't water, but some kind of nutrient broth. There should've been more liquid if that was all the contents in the tube.
Alan quickly issued commands. "Samson, find the ventilation and suck this mist out. We need to see." Something was in those tubes and he needed to know what it was.
"Sir!" The two wounded soldiers accompanied Samson as he looked for a console to hack into. The two would be nothing more than bait as they protected the technologist, but perhaps those few moments would make the difference in the outcome of the mission.
The remaining soldiers tried to gather into a tight formation, but some on the outside couldn't find their way through the mist. Alan heard a cry behind him and turned around. The soldier disappeared through the fog and appeared a moment later on the wall. He seemed to be suspended in midair. Alan would've thought he was levitating if it weren't for the three round holes in his abdomen and the jerking of his body. Alan looked closely and could make out some distortion in front of the soldier, in the shape of?fangs? He couldn't be sure; whatever it was, it was camouflaged. The soldier shuddered one more time as something collided with his back. He stopped twitching and was still. Blood dripped down his pants, but not enough from a wound that size. Most of the blood was going elsewhere. 
The blood flowed through a pattern in midair that curved up and down, traveling outward as if filling up a tube. The blood spread. It began to trace the outline of veins and arteries and four beating hearts. These disappeared as the blood made its way to the surface of the creature, filling in a series of segmented sections with red. As the blood coursed through it, the clear form settled into the shape of a leech. Three fangs surrounded its mouth. It fed on the soldier, drinking its blood, blood that colored it from the inside out.
Alan and the rest of the platoon opened fire. The leech's blood, mixed with their comrade's, spilled out. The leech let out an ear-piercing screech and fell to the ground, disappearing into the mist.
"Where's my air?!" Alan yelled. He couldn't risk firing blindly into the mist when he didn't know everyone's position.
"Working on it, sir," Samson responded. A scream came through his com and was echoed in real life. One of Samson's guards disappeared. "Beck! Where are you?!" Samson called out to his fallen teammate.
"Focus soldier. I need those fans up yesterday!" Alan said.
"Yes sir." He was shaken, but Samson would do his job. He executed more commands through his portable light console. "Found it," he reported.
Alan heard a whirring and the fans mounted into the ceiling started up. They twisted the mist into small tornadoes that spun into nothing as they disappeared. The room cleared. The emptied tubes and dead soldiers appeared on the ground. The first leech was nothing more than a deflated carcass leaking blood. Its head was still twitching.
Argus caught movement out of the side of his eye. It shimmered for a second then was gone. But it left a trail of slime behind it, which another soldier had the misfortune of stepping into. His foot was immediately glued to the metal grating of the floor. Another shimmering as the leech doubled back. The soldier raised his rifle. Its muzzle flashed as he shot bullets that disappeared into the distortion. Alan thought he had hit the leech, but it was unfazed and kept coming forward. Three fangs sank through his torso and came out his back, pinning him in place. The leech's mouth, concentric circles of razor sharp teeth, cut through his clothing, skin and bone. It was a quick but painful death.
Argus pulled out a launcher and loaded a blue-tipped canister into it. "Explosive!" he called out. He pulled the trigger and everyone ducked. The round arced through the air then disappeared into the mass that was starting to gain color. It punctured its flesh with a wet popping noise. He could still see it inside the leech, the little light on its side blinking ever faster. Argus turned away, covering his ears with his hands.
Argus felt the ground shudder. The shockwave of the detonation was dampened by the innards of the leech, but it still coated him in a layer of slime and blood. With a push of his legs he got up and surveyed his work. There was nothing left except for a charred crater.
Now that he could finally see, Alan made out the four tubes that stood in the room. They were all empty of their contents, still leaking goo that dripped from broken glass. Two down. Two left.
Aki saw the third one before anyone else, hiding in the shadows. It had already feasted on blood, so it only had darkness for cover. As soon as Aki spied it, the leech spun to face them. It didn't just leave a trail of slime on the floor; it spewed the clear goo out onto a wide swath of the floor. There was no approach from the front. It slithered along the wall, its movements erratic as it caught the scent of blood from the remaining injured soldier near Samson.
Aki gave herself a running start and put her hand on the sword handle. When she had enough speed, she leapt onto the wall and ran two steps across it. With her sword gripped in both hands, she angled the blade out to her left and pushed off from the wall.
There was barely any resistance. The sharp edge was a trait of Auroran steel. The katana she wielded was known as kujiki, meaning "eater of suffering" in her native tongue. When it cut, the target felt no pain as it was sent from this world of suffering into the next. The leech felt nothing.
She landed. Behind her, the leech fell apart into two halves that slid away from each other with a squelch. She whipped off the gore with a flick and returned kujiki to its sheath.
One more remained. Alan didn't care where it was as long as it wasn't a threat. "Go now!" He pointed to the stairs that led to the upper level. The remaining soldiers scrambled up the steps. Sylvan came up last as he ushered everyone else in front of him.
The upper level was no more than a thin walkway that circled the perimeter of the room, allowing a different view of the broken test tubes and carnage below. After they all made it up, Alan led the way towards the observation deck where they last saw Vijera. If she went out the back, there was a way out of here that led deeper into the facility.
As they were running and about to leave the chamber, Alan noticed that the light on the ground was uneven. It quavered for a moment. He didn't have to look up to know that it was the lamp above him that was moving.
"Back!" he yelled. He had made it to the corner and jumped to the side, but the soldier behind him wasn't as fast. Something clear and wet dropped from the light. The soldier was engulfed by the leech as it landed on top of him. His head disappeared as he collapsed under its weight. Alan and those behind the fallen soldier opened fire, shooting at it until it was nothing more than bullet-riddled gelatin.
Before they could suffer any more casualties, Sylvan guided everyone into the observation room. They gave the body of the leech as much room as they could as they ran by it.
Alan slammed the door behind them and breathed. He did a quick count of the survivors. They had lost half their number again in the last attack. Of the platoon, Sylvan, Deacom and another soldier remained, along with Samson. Alan didn't know how the man did it, but the technologist had a penchant for survival.
Alan checked behind Argus in case he was blocking her, but Aki was gone. Alan knew that according to her contract with the Einhart, she would lend her skills in a fight, but her first priority had always been Vijera and she would take her leave when necessary. But Vijera was a royal and despite Aki's skill, Alan didn't know if she could handle her by herself.
"Vijera released those biologics from here," Alan said. "There has to be some kind of terminal that's connected to the rest of the facility network." The light console was full of buttons and switches. He turned to Samson. "Do you still have your data mine?" 
Samson dug through his pack, his hands slipping on three different colors of gore. He pulled out a data mine, similar to the one Alan had. "Found it, sir," he said.
"I don't know what you'll find, but get it onto the mine and keep it safe," Alan said. "Any biologic information would be useful, so their deaths won't be in vain."
Video streamed from security cameras spread out around the facility. Alan looked for Aki across the series of images. There was at least one body in every screen. The fren still wandered the corridors. It was in a large manufacturing floor with half-made darkspace engines that he found Aki. She was running towards another figure before she disappeared from the edge of the frame.
Alan pointed at the screen. "Where is that?"
"Accessing camera locations. Looks like there's a map, sir. Forwarding to you." Finally, a holographic diagram of the facility popped up from Alan's wrist and floated in front of him. He studied the route they took. "We should've gone left," he murmured.
The Abyssinia Research Station was divided into two main divisions. One half was dedicated to light, space, and darkspace based technologies, while the other half focused on genetic manipulation and biological weapon creation. They were in one of many biologic testing chambers located in the wrong half, but thankfully the layout of the facility was well-planned. It wouldn't take too long to get to the other side. 
"Can you access their darkspace research from this console?" he asked.
Samson shook his head.
"Then find me a terminal that can."
Samson marked a room near the bottom of the facility. "This looks like the main one, sir. You'll have to travel through the darkspace experimentation zones." He marked the path with a yellow line.
"Understood. And where's this screen?" He pointed to the video where he last saw Aki.
"Here," Samson said, pointing to a manufacturing plant. "It's a little past your target terminal."
"Got it." Alan looked at the group. "Sylvan, take your remaining men and guard Samson while the data mine is siphoning. We have separate orders from the High Adjudicant."
"Sir!" Sylvan saluted, hitting the wound across his forehead and causing it to bleed a little bit. The man was dedicated.
"Keep me updated with your progress." Alan nodded at Argus and the two followed Aki's trail.

Chapter 25

Aki ran faster than any soldier, Einhart, Sanctum or otherwise. But Vijera was not a soldier. She was a royal, the product of a lineage designed specifically to be better than everyone else. And she had merged with Aki's sister, Rin. As she ran with one hand on her sword, Aki thought about her sister, more than she had in a long time.
On Aurora, Rin Fushimi was feared even among her own people, a people that used death as a currency. It wasn't her skills that they feared, though those were effective in their own right. It was her creativity.
Their training began when Aki was two. Born from the same parents, Rin was a year older than Aki. But it was Auroran tradition for guilds to remove selected children from their parents before attachment could be formed. The children carried the name and honor of their families, but love had no place in assassinations. An added benefit was that children were easier to train when they weren't the masters' own children. Their teachers started by holding their heads under freezing water every morning. The training began with thirty seconds, with more time gradually added. Sometimes the children died, but that was to be expected. The ones that survived moved on.
Even from an early age, Aki felt a bond with Rin. They competed with each other, out of both sororal duty and their desire to outshine one another in their masters' eyes. But when they left the training grounds, after both were bruised and beaten, either by each other or by their masters after they made a mistake, they bonded as only sisters could. There was something odd about two females who were so talented in a world dominated by men. The boys could never land a blow on them with their wooden training swords and they soon learned that girls that made it as far as the Fushimi sisters did were stronger than everyone else.
Though the personal time the sisters had was precious, they spent it in each other's company. There was no wheedling, no politics, no desire to shame the other. They would sit on the edge of a pond with their feet dangling in the water as they applied ointments to the blue and black splotches that covered their bodies. While rubbing the pungent creams in, they would laugh at the raccoon-dogs that fell into the pond when they chased dragonflies.
As the years passed, they blossomed into two beautiful women that graduated years ahead of their peers. And when they grew into adults, even the masters themselves were afraid of what the sisters could do together. It was then that Rin, the eldest, was given the first sword of the Fushimi clan, murakumo, or "gathering clouds," so named because of the rare white metal used to fashion its blade, making it almost weightless and giving it the power to cut through flesh as if parting air. Aki, the second child, was given kujiki.
In time, each sister grew into her own. Aki was more skilled in marksmanship, excelling in long-distance shooting. She knew how the air felt, how it would affect the bullet, where the target would be when the bullet arrived. And so she was contracted for many stealth missions, like executing unassisted kinetic bombardments from orbit. Her precision also applied to her pressure point techniques. She immobilized and killed her marks with her control of their nervous system and blood flow.
Rin excelled in close quarters combat. It was amazing what she could do with two arms and two legs. With the addition of a blade she had no equal, and she often had Aki under her training sword. She mastered the martial techniques passed down within the Fushimi clan and merged those into the training styles she was exposed to from her own missions.
After they left Aurora, they didn't see each other often. Their skills took them to different places across distant worlds, and it was rare for two agents to work together. Aki became involved with the Einhart, taking down agents of the empire from afar. Rin's skills were better suited to the Sanctum, where brute force and invasion required deft skill on the ground. Yet, their paths rarely crossed on the battlefield, and if they did, the other would make just enough accommodations so that the other could escape.
Vijera, one of the younger princesses of the empire, grew up watching Rin work. She not only coveted the assassin's beauty, but also idolized her abilities. Vijera glorified the way Rin seemed to dance across the battlefield as men and their machines fell, useless, to the ground beneath her. And, as a princess, it was only natural for Vijera to get what she wanted. She was secretive and plotted often, never consulting with advisors or asking for help, always preferring to do things her own way.
The mission she set up for Rin had been simple, a contract drafted for a minor noble of an underpopulated but resource-rich world. Pin the murder on another member of the nobility with planted evidence. Sew political dissent to facilitate Sanctum takeover. Rin took the job.
The poisoned food at the gala event was a success. The target took two bites and started turning green. Not just a sickly green, but a bright emerald green associated with a toxin indigenous to the planet. It was supposed to be public and visible, and the target gave a good performance, leading to a series of tables overturned as his airways swelled shut and he collapsed on the floor. Foam frothed out of his mouth, and Rin, dressed as a server, checked his pulse, or lack thereof, and quickly went to get help by exiting the building through the back, traveling to the extraction point and entering her own shuttle, in preparation for off-world launch. But the oxygen that was supplied for space travel had been replaced by a sleeping gas. As soon as she left the atmosphere, it started filtering through the small cockpit. The only ones who knew how to tamper with an Auroran ship without making it self-detonate was another Auroran, and she and Aki had made enough enemies on their homeworld that she didn't know where to start before she blacked out. The last thing she remembered was her shuttle being guided into a Sanctum imperial ship.
Vijera was ecstatic that she had finally caught Rin. They took her from the shuttle, placed murakumo aside and removed all of the poisons she carried so she wouldn't have a chance to commit suicide.
Rin was one of many contracted assassins and her disappearance wasn't noticed on Aurora until she didn't respond to any of the next ten proposed missions. But by then Vijera had already taken her and wrapped the two of them in a cocoon.
The merge began well. Vijera felt Rin's muscles being added to her own. Short black hair darkened her own auburn hair. Her arms lengthened as they absorbed Rin's limbs. She took on the appearance of a grown woman though she was only a child in mind. The knowledge of the body as a weapon, the poisons from different worlds, and the technology Aurora had that wasn't available to either the Einhart or the Sanctum was now hers, and by extension, the empire's.
But Vijera was greedy. Not only did she want her physical skills and beauty, she wanted all of Rin, including the memories the Auroran held dearest to her.
She tried to gain access to those memories, to the one where Rin and Aki sat with their feet dangling off a bridge as they fed fish in the pond, fat carp with wide eyes and even wider mouths. It was then that Rin struck, hiding the full power of her consciousness within those most precious memories. Vijera was overwhelmed by the sheer fury and force of a betrayed assassin. The cocoon turned black and the two were at risk of dying in moments, which was Rin's intention. The quick-thinking servants saved the cocoon by putting it into stasis as they returned to the throneworld, Sanctuary. It was only with the emperor's power that the merge was completed and Vijera came out in control of the body. But the emperor's power faded over time as Rin, ever vigilant, fought against the shackles that bound her to Vijera's mind. Vijera had no choice but to remain close to the emperor so her father could assist her.
Rin could not undo the merge and refused to control this mix of two bodies that included the princess's subpar mind. She would kill them both and save her dignity and honor. She would save what few Auroran secrets remained that Vijera and the emperor had not yet gained access to. But it grew harder, for the longer she remained bound to this form, the less she remembered of herself, and the emperor removed little pieces of her every time they returned to Sanctuary.

***

Aki continued forward with Rin in her mind as she chased Vijera through the corridors. She passed by a terminal filled with darkspace figures and equations. She threw a location dart at the console as she dashed through, marking it for Alan and Argus. After another set of stairs that spiraled upward, she stepped out onto a long and narrow bridge. Aki could make out Vijera's fleeing form at the other end. Without stopping, Aki pulled out her pistol, synchronized the sight with her running, and fired. The bullet sped straight for Vijera's back, but when it was supposed to impact, Vijera disappeared. The bullet continued and hit the back wall. Vijera materialized a moment later, facing Aki.
"Has your body become as slow as your mind?" Vijera said. 
Though it was dim, Aki was trained to find details in the darkness. Vijera's suit was Sanctum-made, tight and black, similar to Aki's. But there was a purple trim on it, denoting the color only Sanctum royalty could wear. Vijera's hair was longer and a dark shade of brown. But Aki knew those eyes. They were shaped like crescent moons and double lidded, a trait of the Fushimi clan. The sneer, however, was completely Vijera's. Why did she run here? Aki thought. Was there a trap waiting to be sprung on this bridge? Were there more leeches or fren lurking on the other side? 
The bridge had no ceiling. The glass panes that doubled as barriers overlooked darkspace drive production below. This thin bridge must've led between the processing plants. Here, Aki had the advantage. She could fire as many bullets downrange as she wanted while Vijera, it seemed, carried no projectile weaponry. Violence of action would be rendered useless from Rin's training, but Aki could fire.
With two pistols, she compensated for Vijera's increased speed and reflexes. She shot in a wide spread, her left hand shooting at where Vijera was, her right hand shooting at where Aki guessed she would appear next.
The bullets went where she aimed but hit only air. Instead of Vijera, they crashed into the panes of glass that surrounded the bridge. Aki didn't flinch as they shattered. Bits of crystal rained down onto the engine containment fullerenes below. Each time Aki caught a glimpse of Vijera, the lady would turn and disappear, leaving only a shadow in her place. Aki tried to follow with her eyes but couldn't keep up. She now knew how fast she herself appeared to everyone else.
But Vijera was getting sloppy. Her dodges became predictable. Aki fired one more bullet. It sped toward the spot Vijera was materializing in.
Vijera appeared. Aki waited for the sound of impact, for the splatter of blood, but it never came. There was a flash of white light. The bullet split in half and two harmless sparks flashed from the wall behind Vijera. Murakumo had been drawn.
To test if it was truly the blade given to her sister, Aki emptied the last of her clips. Vijera spun the blade with her wrist and fingers, turning it as if it weighed no more than the air she wove around it. Its white metal shone in the darkness as she swept aside all the rounds. The final pane of glass stood behind her, reflecting both of them. Aki stood with her empty guns still aimed at her. Vijera stopped spinning and held her katana horizontally. Murakumo grew darker as silver streaked across its surface. Aki made out the faint metallic rivers that ran across the blade, and even she could barely believe it.
She dropped her guns. "You turned murakumo into a harmonic blade?"
"I made it better," Vijera said. "You Aurorans are still stuck in the past, clinging to tradition as the worlds pass you by." She laughed and with a snap of her wrist flicked the silver liquid behind her. The melted bullets spilled across the glass, sending cracks that branched along its surface as the metal cooled. She raised her sword to the pane of glass. Though it never made contact, the glass shattered nonetheless, leaving the sides of the bridge completely open. Only support beams rose around the edges.
Aki realized she had lost her advantage. Judging by the speed it melted the bullets, Vijera's harmonic blade vibrated in the terahertz range. The vibrations meant that accuracy wasn't necessary; the blade cut through materials by conducting the vibrations through the air immediately surrounding it. The molecular stresses were too much for most materials; their atomic bonds were broken by the energy created from the sword.
They were now in Rin's territory, in Vijera's territory. Aki had spent the last of her bullets trying to defeat an enemy that couldn't be harmed by them. Aki took the sniper rifle off her back and put it onto the ground; it would be useless at this range. She would need to be light if she hoped to survive.
She drew kujiki. "Murakumo is the first sword of my family," Aki said. "It was perfect before you added what was not needed." Her voice was calm and still, like the waves before a storm.
"Don't you mean our family, sister?" Vijera spoke with Rin's voice. When Aki heard it, a part of her broke away and was lost. Aki would never be able to untangle the sound of Rin's voice with the body she was now trapped in. Any hope she might've had of rescuing her sister ceased to exist, like a spark swallowed by the night. She raised kujiki, feeling the familiar weight of her blade.
Vijera changed her stance in response. Her voice was high-pitched and fragile again. "I want her to see this," she said to Aki. "I want Rin to see all of this, the fall of the bastion where she keeps her most precious memories. I will embarrass you, sully the image your sister still has of you, until nothing is left but your battered corpse. She will see it. And when she does, she will break and your sister will be no more."
Aki charged forward. She held her blade out to her side, not wanting to reveal where she would be striking from. The first technique she thought of was the dragon's tail, normally used against multiple targets. She lunged forward, aiming for Vijera's heart. As expected, by the time the blade arrived, it pierced only empty space. Aki executed the second part of the technique. Extending her arm and pivoting on her foot, she swung kujiki in a horizontal arc and spun around, clearing the area immediately around her. Her foot crunched on the glass as she turned. She knew she chose the right opening when her sword was stopped halfway as it impacted with murakumo.
Kujiki shook in Aki's grip as the vibrations transferred from one sword to another. The Auroran steel held and kujiki didn't shatter. A lesser blade would have disintegrated in seconds, but the atomic bonding employed by the craftsmen of her homeworld ensured it would remain whole.
Vijera was forced to parry Aki's strike. She retaliated by drawing the blade up, aiming at Aki's exposed trunk.
Aki dropped to the ground as fast as her muscles could pull her. Even though the blade didn't touch her, the vibrations it sent through the air cut through her suit and skin. A diagonal gash appeared across her stomach, quickly filling with blood. Before the pain could register, from her crouch she switched her grip on kujiki and thrust at Vijera's torso.
But Vijera was gone and Aki hit nothing. The force of her thrust pulled her upright. She looked for the lady. Vijera was mid-leap above Aki, her feet pressed against one of the thin beams that once supported the glass. Her body was tilted towards Aki and she stared like a bird watching its prey. Aki recognized the pose immediately; she knew the tension in the thighs, the sword held off to the side, the adjustments Vijera's feet were making as they made minute changes as she reached the apex of her jump. She would push off in a moment. The diving falcon was a move Aki herself used, and the only effective defense against it was to never let it happen. 
Aki grabbed a shard of glass from the floor and threw it at Vijera's foot on the beam. It flew like a dart and shattered harmlessly, making the beam ring out like a bell. But Vijera was forced to move her foot and the diving falcon would miss its mark. Yet Vijera was confident. She pushed off anyway, her lust for blood overriding Rin's knowledge and experience. The tension in her thighs released in one burst as she dove, aiming for Aki's neck with murakumo.
Aki didn't know what to make of Vijera's judgment, but used it to her advantage. She maneuvered kujiki into position. The tip was pointed not at Vijera's center of mass, but at the much smaller target of her palm. Aki would have to be hit the exact point where she held murakumo and weaken her grip on the sword. But once Vijera pushed off the beam she was at the mercy of gravity. Her course was set.
Aki moved her neck an inch to the right, avoiding murakumo and its harmonic vibrations. She felt the tip of her sword touch Vijera's hand. In position, she held it tightly and let Vijera's momentum do the rest.
Vijera landed behind her. Four fingers followed, dropping to the ground with wet thuds, some still twitching atop the broken glass and staining it red. Murakumo fell from Vijera's broken grip; its blade still hummed and sank through the floor of the bridge, held in place only by the handle that swung back and forth.
Aki wasn't finished yet. Rin was too close and Aki didn't have time to turn around or change grips. She dropped her own sword and extended the pointer and middle fingers of both hands. There would be no time for needles.
With Vijera's back turned to her, she quickly hit the key pressure points she needed to damage. She shot forward like a snake, her fingers jabbing with such speed that it looked like she had six hands. 
She hit the point above the scapula for left arm control and the point between lumbar four and five for shoulder rotation. Yet with each strike something didn't feel right.
