She could compensate for minor differences from body to body, but Vijera no longer conformed to human anatomy. It seemed like her muscles were denser, her cartilage thicker and her joints were slightly misaligned. Each of these differences rendered Aki's attacks useless. Even though she struck with precision for two seconds, the normal changes of the stiffened neck and loss of muscle control didn't occur. She realized the futility of her attacks and went to grab kujiki, but Vijera had already recovered. As soon as Aki gripped her sword, Vijera swung out with her fists balled into a club and smashed them into Aki's chest.
A line of blood shot out from the cut on Aki's stomach. She felt ribs crack as she was sent flying. Strands of her hair fluttered in front of her eyes. The force of the blow would send her off the bridge. She looked to her right and was about to fly past one of the support beams. She pulled her legs back and hooked onto it. The crook of her knees acted as a pivot as she swung around. After a revolution, she extended them, letting go, using her remaining speed to propel her back onto the bridge. She landed on her back and slid a few feet. Through the pain, she held onto kujiki. She thrust it into the ground to slow herself. Glass built up behind her as she stopped.
Vijera's right hand was reduced to a thumb and four bleeding stumps, so she picked up murakumo with her left hand.
Though Aki had trouble breathing, she stood up as well. She tried to straighten her back but the pain was too great. Slouching was bearable, and the pressure helped keep the bleeding in check.
The sisters faced each other and prepared to dance again.

Chapter 26

"Aki!" Alan called out as he ran past darkspace engine enclosures. He looked at his map. Aki should've been close by. Another blip came from the room they were approaching, this time from one of Aki's personal markers.
Argus followed close behind, his guns sweeping for invisible leeches and fren that might jump out from the shadows. None had appeared, yet he was afraid Alan's calling would attract them to their position. But he kept silent. He wanted to find Aki just as much as he did. She wasn't a member of the Einhart, but she was still a member of their team. They would find her.
Alan trotted up the stairs to the room Aki marked. Argus backed up each step one at a time, scanning for any movement. When he saw none, he hurried and caught up with Alan. Perhaps they didn't roam this area since there wasn't any prey.
The door they passed through had already been opened. They found the location dart Aki had left them on top of a wide console. It was small and thin, barely larger than the needles Aki used to cure Alan of his joint pains. But it emitted a powerful signal on a specific frequency, and Alan knew she had passed through here.
Alan looked up at the projections surrounding him. Symbols he had never seen before mixed with symbols he knew. They danced across the screen, reforming themselves into endless permutations as if they tried to solve a riddle.
The console appeared locked down, but Alan wasn't concerned with access. He brought out the data mine, designed by Dr. Guren and given to him by Claudia. It was still suspended inside its magnetic cage, untouched and undamaged throughout all the fighting. He reached down into the thin cylinder, gingerly pinched the data mine with two fingers and brought it out. The magnetic field that suspended it dissipated. Though it was the biggest the Einhart had ever made, it looked the same size as all the other ones he had used in the past; a metallic cone that tapered into a sharp point that fit in his palm. He fiddled with the top of it, trying to twist it or pull it so the bundle of wires and programs inside would activate. It worked before with previous models on past missions.
Nothing happened. He was about to just smack it really hard against the console and see if that would fix it, but at the last moment he decided against it. "Argus! Do you know how to work this thing?" he asked.
Argus slung his rifle over his shoulder and held out his hand, beckoning with his fingers in mock impatience. "Didn't bother to read the instructions?" he joked. 
Alan gave him the data mine. It looked even smaller in Argus's hand. "Just turn it on."
"I mean really, boss, we had a whole darkspace flight to figure it out," he quipped as he studied the console. A look at Alan's steely face and Argus grinned. "Here," he said. He found a projection unit on the console, where light streamed out of a tiny aperture that formed an image of a darkspace engine prototype. With a thrust, he shoved the sharp part of the data mine into it, breaking the aperture's glass.
Alan was afraid the data mine was nothing more than scrap metal after that, but when Argus backed away, small lights began to glow on the part of the cone that stuck out from the aperture. "Well," he said with his hands on his hips. "I could've done that."
The lights were accompanied by a high-pitched whirring. Wires of every color shot out. Each was tipped with a metal spike on its end. The wires were like threads, thin and flexible. They dashed this way and that, tearing through the metal beneath the light console as if it were no more than paper. The ends pierced the circuitry inside, hijacking the information and siphoning it into the data mine. The wires climbed over each other in an effort to commandeer all the data. In a few moments, the tangle of rainbow-colored vines had covered it so completely that barely any of the silver console was visible. All the wires led back to the data mine. The core spike started to drain the network.
The data was uploaded to Alan's wristband and projected in front of him. Equations, schematics, and unfinished projects flashed by one after the other. The data spike prioritized darkspace propulsion information, and he was looking at a new engine shell when Argus tapped him on the back. It was a touch so light that Alan knew something was wrong as soon as he felt it. He didn't ask but only turned around and looked questioningly. Alan saw nothing so he quieted the beating of his heart, ignored the whirring of the data spike in the background. Then he heard it. The quiet clicks of claws against the concrete floor, the tiny squeaks as the darkspace engine frames were gently nudged aside. Fren? The clicks continued across the ground, untraceable. They were far but getting closer.
The quiet was shattered by the gunshot reports ringing out high above them, followed by a rain of glass. The sudden burst of sound jolted both of them. "Aki," Alan said.
"Go. I'll defend and hold until it's finished," Argus said, nodding to the data mine that chirped as it guzzled information.
Alan turned to go, but Argus stopped him.
"It's dangerous to go alone," the big man said with a twinkle in his eye. "Take this." Argus held out his grenade launcher, the same one he used on the leech earlier.
"You'll need it for the fren," Alan said, trying to push it back.
"I have something I can use here," he said, looking around at the engine frames. "This is the only way in and better defended with something else," he said, pointing to the wide doorway they had come through. "I've never heard Aki miss before. You'll need it." Argus dropped the launcher. Alan caught it with both hands and hefted it across his shoulder. He handed the suspension case for the data mine to Argus, nodded his thanks and went up the staircase.
Argus turned back as Alan's footsteps faded above him. The data mine would require more time to complete its work as it sifted through the databases in the research facility. 
He quickly set up his perimeter. His bag, or mobile armory as he thought of it, had become light. Not much was left. Dumping out the remainder of its contents onto the floor, he sifted through spare ammo and sealed silver packages before he found what he was looking for. His hand closed around a bandolier with black canisters attached to it. Each canister held three balls made of soft, malleable plastic. He pulled one out. It immediately changed its color and patterning to match the textured grip of his gloves. He aimed the camouflage mine, or k-mine as the other soldiers called it, and threw it as hard as he could back towards the way they had come, past the stairs and out onto the manufacturing level. It bounced once and changed colors as it rolled across the floor, calculating color distribution and adding cracks based on observed patterns. When it came to a rest, the ball began melting. It thinned and spread out across the ground until it was only a few millimeters thick, blending into the floor like a concrete pancake. Its new shape activated the explosive charge. Its pressure sensors would be tested soon enough.
Argus threw more k-mines out across the floor. As they flattened and activated, their locations were yellow arrows that appeared from his wrist projection. The area was wide and he didn't have enough to cover the whole floor, but this would have to do. 
He grabbed the silver packets he set aside earlier and ripped them open. It looked like there was nothing inside, but once the nanomachines were exposed to air, they came to life and flew out in a buzzing cloud. The object they were going to create had already been programmed into them; Argus had seen to its design and engineering during the trip from the Aurelius to Abyssinia. He hoped that his calculations had been correct and that they could use the material here. What he wanted to make wouldn't be as easy as a chair. It needed a lot of metal. 
The nanomachines knew to avoid the data mine behind them and went out onto the manufacturing floor to search for the proper materials. They scanned the concrete and air for the necessary materials, but those didn't have what they needed. They only found a match when they hit upon the darkspace engine frames, made of an alloy designed to withstand the pressures of interdimensional travel. Using their lasers, they cut off atomic chunks of the material and brought it back to the assembly area designated behind the wide doorway. After one trip they only had part of one of the support struts built. Argus knew there wasn't enough time. 
He ventured out from the doorway, his rifle held at the ready. He avoided the yellow areas marked on his map, ever vigilant for a stray tail or glowing red eye. He didn't see any as he reached the first fullerene engine cage. With a yank it came off the hooks that held it on the assembly line. It was heavy. Even with his strength, he had to drag it across the floor. If those creatures didn't know where he was, the screech of the metal against the floor made his position clear. He reached another one and pulled it off with his other hand. As it came crashing to the ground, the first mine exploded far away. Argus scanned the dim edges of the production plant. A large plume of orange and red lit up around the furthest k-mine. 
From behind, within the supposed safe zone, he heard the click clack of claws against concrete. One had made it through, and judging by the rhythm, it was already too close. There was no time to release the engine cages and grab his rifle, so he extended his arms, turned his feet and swung around in a circle, using the cages as extensions of his fists. As he started his second revolution, one of the cages crashed into something hard, almost jolting the cage from his grip. He caught a glimpse of something toothy and shaggy batted away between spins. The fren must've landed on another mine. Another plume of fire went up where it fell. He felt the shockwave and heat on his back.
He shuffled back and dragged the cages up the stairs. A low growl was the only warning he got before a fren leapt from the shadows and ran towards him. With the adrenaline coursing through his veins, he pulled the cages to either side of himself. The fren was coming closer, but he waited. After it leapt, when Argus could see the drool leak out of its mouth, he flexed his chest and smashed the cages together. The fren was sandwiched between the bars. The force of Argus's attack destroyed the scaled armor and the flesh underneath it. He felt bones break as metal rang against metal. The fren slid off the rods in pieces.
He finished climbing the stairs with the gore-covered darkspace engine shells. The nanomachines assembled faster now that the source material was in front of them; the other support legs started to take shape.
With the time the mines bought, Argus checked on the data mine. Only halfway done. He started to loathe that little bar that wouldn't fill faster. Its colorful spinning, designed to soothe the senses, only stoked the fire of his impatience more. He turned back to guard his domain.
His rifle would have to do until the nanomachines were finished. He opened the last canister of k-mines. With precise throws, he placed the three mines in a row leading up to the stairs. He was the king of this hill, and until the data mine had taken the data it needed, he would keep it safe.
The mines on the outer perimeter detonated. He kept watch through the yellow markers projected in front of him. Four markers disappeared in quick succession, followed by four fireballs on the right. Argus could make out large metal sphere cages fly into the air. When he looked closely there were bits of fren and perhaps something else. 
Argus guessed that they would approach from the center or the left now, scared off by the explosions on the right. There was only one set of stairs that led up to him, and Argus commanded a clear view and the high ground. But he was only one person, and he didn't know how long he could last.
Another series of explosions. The center cluster detonated. They were closer now. Argus could make out their triplet red eyes as they covered the open ground that led to the stairs.
While they were still far off, Argus looked into his scope and picked them off one by one. He didn't have Aki's accuracy or her sniper rifle, but he managed to hit a few of them. The more densely grouped mines closer to the base did the rest. He slapped in a fresh magazine and fired again, always keeping an eye on the nanomachines. Enough of the weapon had been built so that he could balance his rifle on top of one of its shield plates, improving his aim and reducing recoil.
The fren came in waves. They were close; Argus didn't need to look through his sight to aim. He counted at least ten of the creatures as they galloped towards his position. He kept pulling the trigger until the gun clicked uselessly. He had spent the last of his ammunition. 
The nanomachines had deconstructed the two darkspace engine cages. All that was left of them were scraps of metal deemed unfit for the project. In their place stood another pile of metal in the same color, but arranged into thin, triangular flaps on the floor. The rest of the weapon was a stationary turret, with heavy front shielding made of sheets of alloy. Argus put his hands on the two grips, still being finished between his fingers. Two power cords snaked back towards the console and connected with the facility's power supply. His display told him that the current was flowing with minimal fluctuations. The railgun was active. Large ammo shells derived partly from the cages and partly from the atoms in the air hung in a long line below the main body of the railgun. New shells were being made from available materials, their cylindrical bodies only half-formed, but they would diminish in quality as materials became scarce. These first rounds were the best.
Argus turned the railgun on. The electric current surged into the gun, and the triangular flaps on the ground floated upwards. They arranged themselves into a long cylinder held together by magnetic force. Tendrils of green electricity crackled between each of the pieces. They wobbled slightly, as if they were being blown by a light breeze.

"Even my life, etched into stone
in time
becomes nothing more than
dust before the wind."

The fren reached the base of the staircase as the first shell was loaded into the railgun. The lights dimmed as the railgun drained power from the facility. The electricity funneled into the cylinder that acted as the barrel of the gun. Argus took aim, leading the fren with his reticle, and fired. Electricity cycled through the railgun; magnetic fields propelled the spinning metal slug out of the muzzle with the smell of ozone and the sound of striking lightning. The slug was shot out so quickly it partially melted as it accelerated towards its target. When it struck, there was no fire, but only a deafening crash as the kinetic energy obliterated everything around it.
When the dust settled, the ground was no more. Where a group of fren used to be only a crater remained. The shell blew through the concrete, and Argus could catch a glimpse of the level beneath the manufacturing plant. 
Bits of concrete pinged off of Argus's armor as the next shell was loaded.
Two more minutes and the data mine would be finished.

Chapter 27

"Aki!" Alan called out as he took the stairs two at a time. The bullets had stopped, replaced by the clashing of steel against steel. The blood pumped in his legs and sweat dripped off his face but he didn't slow. Vijera had become a feared name throughout the Einhart after her merger with Aki's sister, and despite his confidence in Aki, Alan didn't know if they could neutralize her with just the two of them.
When he reached the top of the stairs, the bridge was already listing to one side. Vijera held her sword with her left hand; her movements were sloppy and wide from the pain. Each time she missed, she cut through the floor. With every swing, one of the support beams underneath the bridge was severed by murakumo. It groaned as the two fought above it, weakening it with every step.
Aki wasn't in much better shape. She bled from a gash on her stomach, and Alan was surprised she was still standing considering how much blood was on the floor. She slouched and her breath came in ragged gasps. She managed to parry Vijera's blows with her own sword, but she was weakening and kujiki looked heavy in her grip.
Alan aimed with his rifle but couldn't get a clear shot; they still moved too quickly. He switched to the grenade launcher he received from Argus. With his sight synced to the reticle, he aimed for a far beam. Accuracy wasn't important; all he needed was the concussive blast and enough time to retrieve his teammate. He aimed high and fired.
Aki locked swords with Vijera. She pushed as hard as she could to maintain her footing as she sought for any opening to use against Vijera. With her back turned to him, she didn't know Alan was there until she saw the projectile grenade out of the corner of her eye. But in that moment of distraction, Vijera planted a kick in the middle of Aki's weakened chest, forcing her to the ground. Aki grimaced as she fell. The grenade was still arcing through the air, but Vijera was fast and the blade was coming down on her neck. There was no chance of Vijera missing now, when Aki had nowhere to go. She closed her eyes, regret filling her as she awaited the deathblow.
It never came. When she opened her eyes, Vijera was still poised over her, her mouth tightened into a line full of rage, the tendons sticking out like suspension wires underneath her neck. Her left hand was frozen mid-swing. Aki noticed the change happening in her eyes. They softened, grew deeper. Vijera's mouth relaxed. And for just a moment, Aki saw Rin again. She was on the ground after sparring with her elder sister, just as she had always been. For just a moment, they were two sisters again.
Rin didn't have control over the vocal chords, so she mouthed only one word. "Live." The grenade detonated behind her. She smiled, and then jumped backwards into the explosion. The last thing Aki saw was Vijera's hair on fire and the suit melting and fusing to her skin. Aki reached out a limp hand, as if she could save Rin by pulling her back.
A pair of strong arms grabbed Aki from behind. "No!" Aki shouted, but she was too weak to struggle. Alan dragged her back from the flames, then off of the bridge. They watched as the blast weakened the thin floor and the fire ate through the remaining supports. The metal strained and fought to keep its original shape but couldn't. With a groan, one by one the supports snapped. 
The bridge collapsed. It was a long way down, and the fires burned brightly as Alan and Aki watched the bridge crash onto the engine production level below. Explosions lit up the area as it touched undetonated k-mines. The floor, already weakened by Argus's railgun blasts, caved in as the bridge fell on top of it. The bridge shattered and broke through the ground, finally coming to a rest in pieces, exposing a smaller basement corridor that ran under the production level.
Back to her senses, Aki looked up at Alan. "Do you think she's alive?" she asked. Her voice was weak, but tempered by determination and love.
Alan looked down at the wreckage. He didn't know which answer would hurt less. "I don't know," he decided. "The royals are tough. Like you," he said, smiling. "Can you stand?" 
She nodded and tried to push herself up, but collapsed when her arms couldn't support her. A trail of blood leaked out of her mouth. Alan put his head under her arm and pushed up with his legs. With one arm draped over his shoulders, they made their way down the stairs one at a time. Alan lost his balance twice with the added weight of the grenade launcher and Aki, but he put one foot after the other and that was enough.

***

Argus had run out of regular shells for his railgun. To make new ones, the nanomachines had to resort to the nearest material. Each of the new shells was a mess of biological, metal and concrete components, harvested from the fren corpses and whatever else was close at hand. Each shell, if it could be called that, was covered in a layer of slime. With every shot the railgun deteriorated, its magnetic launching fields only exerting force on the metal components. One even misfired, landing dangerously close to Argus as it exploded in a shower of gore and shrapnel on the stairs as it detonated the remaining mine. He had only ducked behind the defenses of the turret in the nick of time. Something chunky splattered on his back. The railgun wouldn't last much longer.
As he fired the last shell, a particularly malformed and goopy one, he saw Alan with Aki. They tripped over the last stair and barely caught themselves. With no more fren in the immediate vicinity, he went to help them. He took the grenade launcher back from Alan. Aki had to keep her hand on the wall, but she could stand.
"Is it done yet?" Alan asked, nodding to the data mine.
"No, ten seconds," Argus responded. He was back at the railgun, inputting final commands. The barrel of the railgun started spinning faster, a dizzying blur of triangles and green electricity. "Where's our exit?" he asked.
Alan looked to where the bridge had fallen through the floor and into the basement below. "There. The fallen bridge made an opening we can slip through."
"Do you know where it goes?" Argus asked. He aimed the barrel towards the wreckage of the bridge.
"Looks like there are launch tubes that reach the surface," Alan responded.
"What's the time on the EMP?"
"Three seconds."
"Firing." Argus aimed towards the bridge and shot the railgun one last time. There was no ammunition inside its chamber. The barrel of the gun itself launched, leaving only the bottom half of the turret intact. The spinning cylinder of metal shards and electricity flew towards the flames. Behind him, the tangle of cables and wires that extended from the data mine retracted into the core spike, leaving the console looking like a cheese from Cybele. The core spike emitted an electromagnetic pulse. It spread, destroying the circuitry of anything that wasn't shielded. The console it was connected to sparked and fizzled, the cloud of nanomachines fell dead to the ground, and row after row of lights went out. When the pulse reached the barrel flying through the air, the green electric field that held it together dissipated and it spun apart. Triangular sheets of metal unfolded and lodged themselves into fren and concrete and fire, clearing a path for them to get through.
Though electricity was gone, the data mine still glowed brightly. Argus handed him the mine case. Alan pulled the mine out of the console, put it back into its case and stuffed it into his pocket. He commanded it to sort through the data and display anything relevant as they were making their escape.
They climbed down the broken staircase. Alan led the way, jogging across uneven craters. Argus carried Aki, close behind. They ran together, guided by the flames that still burned where the bridge had fallen. Alan was alert, but it seemed nothing else survived.
After crawling over concrete and stepping over bodies, they reached the hole in the floor. Alan jumped down first followed by Argus. Aki groaned slightly as they landed. The lights were still functioning on this level.
They ran down the empty hallway for a short distance until Argus said, "Wait." He gently put Aki down and reloaded the launcher. Facing the way they'd come, he aimed for a cracked spot on the ceiling and launched a grenade.
They continued on before it hit. Chunks of rock and concrete came down, caving in the corridor behind them. A cloud of dust coated their backs. Nothing would follow them down here and they could slow their pace. Now that their mission was over, all they needed was a way out.
Alan looked through the map of the area, trying to figure out where this tunnel led. Further ahead, there were launching tubes that reached the surface, only he didn't know what was being launched from them.
His com chattered to life. "Second Medium, do you copy?" It was Sylvan. "Second Medium, are you safe?"
"Alan here. We're alive."
"Thank the High Adjudicant! We heard explosions come from the way you'd gone and we feared the worst."
"What's your status?" Alan asked.
"The data mine finished its work and we went back the way we came. It's down to Deacom, Samson and myself, but we're safe here. If we open this door we can leave the facility, though I don't know what's waiting for us out there."
"Hold position, I'll find a way to get you-" Alan said, but his attention immediately turned to a strange map that appeared on his display. "It couldn't be," he said to himself. 
"Sir, what is it?" Sylvan asked, but Alan ignored him. He rotated the image. It was an area of space that encompassed at least a dozen solar systems. Overlaid on top of it was a network of passages that connected to each other, like the top of a tree; its branches were directed in certain areas, with smaller twigs ending up at specific worlds. But the map had two different views. One was in normal space, where everything was scattered and didn't make sense, with large gaps between the passages. The map was disjointed, like a single piece of glass that had been shattered. But when he shifted the image into the darkspace view, it all came together into a fluid whole.
It was no wonder why, despite all their searching, they could never find Sanctuary, homeworld of the empire. It took massive amounts of energy to move a ship from normal space to darkspace, but the work he was looking at, the research they did here, made it possible for even a small planet to shift into the other dimension. That planet followed a set path through prefabricated routes invisible to everything else. It pulsed steadily across one of the branches, a beautiful blip of light in the darkness.
"Sanctuary," Alan said. "We've found it."

Chapter 28

Ashra awoke. She didn't open her eyes, but she noticed that the Empyrean, with its island surrounded by clear waters and the tree that stood at its center, had changed. Something was different here, something vibrant and alive. The tree had grown during her time in stasis, now overshadowing her as she sat under it, giving her shade from a sun that didn't exist. Small buds began to bloom into flowers on the tips of the branches. Their scent was calming and light, lending only the faintest trace of perfume to the air. With the exception of the perpetuals, those colored stars that hung in the center of the sky, the other stars had become much fewer in number. They were clustered in the eastern part of her sky, with another grouping to the north. Very few people lived in wherever it was she landed. Yet there was life here, more than she had ever felt before. It was not the life that encompassed individuals. It was nourishing, something from deep under the ground, something that had its own rhythm and mind, something that slept, undisturbed by the events on the surface far above it.
The plush restraints of the escape pod had deflated back to their original shape, allowing Ashra to move again. As she stepped onto the threshold, she let her hand linger across its surface. It seemed silly to still call it an escape pod, something made for only one use. This vessel had journeyed from warship to capital ship and finally settled on land. Though she couldn't see, she knelt down and felt its cold floor, if only to touch her mother's blood that was still there. We made it, she thought, as her fingers ran across something dried and grainy. And though you are no longer in my sky, we shall fulfill your last wish together and see what this world gave you, and why you chose this place for me. For us.
The first thing she noticed as she stepped outside was the smell. She didn't know the word for it because she had never experienced it, but what the wind blew through her was the scent of the earth, the scent of the wildflower pollen and the dirt after the rain. When she stepped down from the pod and the grass tickled her legs under her skirt, she knew what nature was for the first time, what genuine gravity felt like. She was not on a ship with a destination; she was anchored to the ground of a planet drifting through space, one that derived its sustenance not from any engine but from the sun itself.
 And so, without knowing why, she took off her shoes and felt the grass with her feet, feeling each blade slide between her toes. She felt the soft ground underneath them, providing a cushion as she jumped up and down. She smiled, a smile that can only be seen from a child playing in the grass. She sought to find a pair of eyes so she could see what it was that brought her such joy, but the only life she felt close to her was that of the insects and little animals, whose eyesight she could not share. They appeared as cosmic dust in the Empyrean, too small to hold to her eye.
Yet, she was not alone. The stars had changed position and some were closer than before. Her arrival on Phalos had not gone unnoticed. It seemed like the northern stars were coming. These were like no stars she had seen before; their shapes were varied, some were full of points, some were ellipses, and one of them was even round and hollow like a torus.
In all her years on the Edict, the only stars she knew were the round white ones. They would shine brightly. The stars of the soldiers would parade in lines from one end of the Empyrean to the other, and she would compose a small march in her head as they bounced their way across the ship during their drills, twinkling all the way. The officers were sloppier, yet the shape of their stars had always been the same. Humans. They were all humans. Round and bright, that's what they were.
But these, these oblong and strange ones, some parts round and bright, other parts rough and dull; she didn't know what to make of them. The paths of some of them were erratic; they would dash forward then backward, go up and then down, giving the impression they were climbing something. But one of the strange stars remained steady in its path; it looked like three white orbs being pulled to the center by a black hole. Each orb had a long tail that wound the three into a circle.
What were these things? Perhaps they weren't humans; perhaps they were like her, part human, part something else, pariahs of their own societies. She could try to use their eyes, if they had eyes at all, but she didn't know what would happen. Sometimes her targets would become drowsy, other times they'd feel an itch in the back of their mind they couldn't scratch; how would these creatures react? She didn't want to risk linking her mind with something that could possibly use her instead. She would remain blind and rely on herself.
This is where her mother wanted her to be, she thought. Though delayed, this was the spot she was supposed to be in. And these new stars, she had never seen their like before. Were they friend or foe? She dug into the belt to check what she had. The knife Aki had given her, the tracking beacon from Alan, and the pendant with no chain from her mother. She might be able to defend herself.
She didn't know the terrain, so she put her shoes back on and took small steps forward. She still needed a guide, and settled for a branch she found on the ground. She broke it to match her height. There was warmth on her skin from the sun. The wind gently blew her hair as if her mother were behind her, straightening it with a comb made of air. She decided she was in a meadow. A meadow! Something she only saw through her tutors' eyes as she studied. She didn't know how far it extended, but she knew she wanted to walk to its edge.
If it were a week ago, she would've fled. She didn't know who or what was looking for her, and her first instinct had always been to hide, to hide behind her mother, behind her hair, behind a wall. She was the outcast princess, Princess of the Void.
But things had changed and she had changed. She remembered Vijera and the final moments of her mother's star, she remembered the angry men who came to her cell and demanded she return their conquered homeworlds. But most of all, she remembered Alan. She remembered when he first supported her in his strong arms as she left the pod, when he held her as only Lydia had held her before, when he trusted her, and she in turn trusted him. His pale yellow star still shone brightly, and though she didn't know where the Einhart sent him, she took strength from the fact that he was alive.
She was still Ashra, Princess of the Void, but this time she would not hide. With her branch as her guide, she put one foot in front of the other and headed towards the group of strange stars as they danced up and down. As she kept her feet along the narrow path through the meadow, she wondered if her mother had walked the same path she was walking now.

Chapter 29

Again, it was Zava who spotted her first. The runners ran ahead, but their sight was blocked by the tree trunks that cast their shadows over the dirt path. Zava could crawl and climb as easily as he walked, and he found a perch among the trees. The pod had opened. Walking towards them was a small figure. He saw her immediately; how the runners had not noticed her was beyond his comprehension. She had long hair the color of snow and wore white garments so bright she shone like a star in the day.
"There's someone coming!" he called down, his voice full of pride. The runners increased their speed, their legs fueled by competition, to see who could reach her first. The runners were a strange group. Their upper bodies were those of young men and women, older than Zava. Their lower halves had been fused with the legs of a bird that ran on the ground of Phalos. Their heights varied, but the one thing they had in common was that they were fast, and when they were fast, they were silent.
When Zava looked again, the brightness of the figure had been reduced as she entered the shade of the trees. She must've felt it as well, for she slowed as she entered the shadows.
Zava climbed down and walked next to Orana. Though she seemed to be taking a leisurely stroll through the woods, her pace was deceptively quick with her eight legs, each step stirring up puffs of dirt around her. Her eyes, the two human ones and the six spider ones, kept watch for ambush. She walked with her wrists crossed; her hands lay on top of the spider's hairy head, and from the waist up it appeared as if she were being borne by a palanquin, so smooth was her gait. "Who's coming?" she asked. She patted the emblem emblazoned on the abdomen of her spider half, inviting Zava to ride with her. It was rare to have the maiden out of her cave, and Zava relished every moment in her company. With a hop, he sat on the hairy surface, enjoying being rocked back and forth by the movement of the spider underneath him.
"She was all white, like this!" he said, gently pulling on the tuft of white hairs that made up the symbol.
She glanced behind and laughed. "And how many did you see?" she asked.
"Just one." He saw the worried look on her face and quickly added, "but maybe there were others I couldn't see."
"There has never been anything you couldn't see with your bright eyes," she said with a smile. Yet even Zava, only a child, could sense her anxiety. 

***

Ashra felt the sunlight cool to shadow as she followed the path, feeling its curves and edges with her feet and walking stick. The stars came closer, and it seemed like their energetic bouncing had been reduced to little hops as she neared. She showed no fear. More than a princess of the empire, she was her mother's daughter and would walk with the confidence that Lydia had given her.
They met amongst the trees, under their layered shade. Ashra stood alone with her stick. On the other side, the climbers had crawled to the highest branches to provide a lookout. The runners stayed to the side of the path; they knelt down on their elongated feet, like birds roosting on the grass. The Maiden Orana stepped forward with Zava next to her, and they stopped in front of Ashra.
Ashra stood silent, her face upheld and her eyes closed. Orana, as the ambassador for Phalos, spoke first.
"Welcome to Phalos," she said. "We have been awaiting your arrival." She bowed as low as she could, bending at the waist with her human half while the spider lowered its front legs.
Ashra heard the woman's voice and was reminded of her mother. There was something in the timbre, in the curve of the words. No one else had spoken like Lydia before.
Ashra prepared her gestures in advance. She pointed to her throat and shook her head, frowning slightly. With her other hand, she held two fingers to her forehead, tapped the place between her eyes and then extended them towards the voice she heard.
"What does she mean?" Zava asked. Orana watched intently, studying Ashra's face. She didn't know what to make of this young child in white. She looked like Lydia. She had white hair; a trait of the priestesses after they awakened. 
But Orana was cautious. She did not survive this long by trusting others. Who was this child? Why was Lydia not here? She would only get the answers if she opened her mind. "Let us talk," she said.
Ashra nodded. She held the star that stood in front of her, the one made of three white orbs that curled into themselves. She extended her mind, the tendrils of thought tentative and probing. As soon as they reached Orana, Ashra was repulsed by a force, an unyielding wall that pushed her away. She had never felt such a strong resistance against her mind before. The sudden impact jolted her.
 Orana apologized. "I have not been in the presence of one with the powers of a priestess for many years." She closed her eyes and calmed herself, going deep into her own mind, removing the layers of protection that enshrouded it. "Please, try once more," she said.
Ashra sought out the maiden's mind again. Despite having to maneuver around mental obstacles Orana had forgotten to remove, she made contact this time. <I am Ashra,> she said. She considered introducing herself as the Sanctum princess, but thought better of it. <The daughter of Lydia,> she finished.
Orana's face lit up. "It is wonderful news to hear you all have survived. Where is she now? Where is Lydia? Is she still where you landed? Is she hurt?" The words tumbled out.
Ashra shook her head. <Lydia, my mother, is no longer with us. She perished aboard the Edict as we tried to escape.> She reached inside the pocket within her belt and took out a small bundle wrapped in cloth. <This is all I have to remember her by.>
Orana brushed her fingers over the pendant Ashra held. The blue jewel in the center seemed to absorb the light filtered through the trees, lending a glow to the half moon it was ensconced in. "The Moon of Phalos," she said. "It has returned. Truly then, you are her daughter." She pulled back her hand. "And she is gone." Her ebullience had been tempered, but her hope remained strong.
<The Moon of Phalos?> Ashra wrapped it back up and returned it to her belt. <This was a jewel my mother had. She left it with me as we were leaving.>
"Phalos has two moons that circle it, and a third that defies orbit, going only where the priestess goes."
<My mother was a priestess?>
"No, but she was going to be. She was going to be the one Priestess of Phalos, the one who sends. I was her attendant." Orana heard a faint rustling in the leaves. Though it was probably only an animal, she grew anxious. "Let us go somewhere more private, away from the eyes and ears of the empire."
<I understand.> Ashra was going to start walking with her stick again, but asked, <May I borrow a pair of eyes?>
Orana looked confused. "I'm afraid we don't have any spare ones," she said.
Ashra laughed. <I may be blind, but I can see through the eyes of others with my mind, like I can speak with you.>
"Ah. In that case, let me give you the best pair of eyes we have, the ones that first spotted your arrival."
Zava trotted close to them and offered a clawed hand. "Nice to meet you," he said. He and Ashra were about the same height.
Ashra took it. She felt the rough scaly skin, but didn't mind. <Your name?>
Zava grinned as he heard a voice speak not to his ears but to his mind. "I'm Zava, the climber! You need my eyes?"
Ashra nodded. <If you'll allow me.> 
"Uh huh!" He vigorously nodded his consent. Ashra felt the vibrations through his hand.
In the Empyrean, she plucked out the star closest to her, a rounded sliver that looked like a sickle, and put it up to her eye. In that moment, her world changed. She had never seen with such clarity, such brilliance before. Yet more important than the tool through which she saw was what she saw. Only in holograms had she seen trees like these and grass so green.
She caught a glimpse of herself as they trekked deeper into the forest. She walked with confidence now; her back was straight and her head was held high. The runners, on legs made from some large bird, dashed ahead while the climbers gripped trees with clawed hands and feet. She saw the Maiden Orana. The maiden's human half was quiet and gentle, while her spider half was feral and proud.
As if sending her thoughts, Orana asked, "You are wondering what happened here, aren't you?"
<A little,> she admitted. <If this is the natural order here, I only wonder why my mother and I don't look like you.>
"This," she said, gesturing to herself, "happened after your mother was taken. Before the arrival of the Sanctum, we were all human. We had settlements and enjoyed our lives here, living off the bounty of the land. So it had been as far back as we could remember, and so we thought it would always be."
They continued deeper into the forest. To make better time, one of the runners put Ashra upon Orana's back. "Our clan has always been a matriarchy, ruled by women," she continued. "And the greatest of these women was the priestess. Only she was capable of the sending, where the souls of the dead are returned to the earth."
<Souls? The people of Phalos believe in souls?>
"You do not?" Orana asked.
<I do not. Souls are a religious construct. We are a bundle of nerves and electric impulses. If there is a soul, then it is only a representation of who we believe we are,> Ashra said.
Orana laughed. "How does one as young as you come to such a conclusion?"
<Time passes slowly in my mind.>
"And in your mind, is there a field of stars from which you see the world?"
Ashra was surprised. <How did you know?>
"You are not the first descendant of the priestess I have known. After the empire arrived, I lost my faith as well. Souls may be just as you describe them. But that blueprint of consciousness, perhaps an electrical impulse, perhaps a soul, perhaps an essence of life; regardless of what it is called, it is powerful. Our priestess then, Izmir, wielded their power, turning our dead and the Sanctum dead into weapons. She turned them into blooms of light that eclipsed the two moons of Phalos and stretched to the stars, where their ships came plummeting to the ground when they touched the branches. Our weapons were primitive but adequate."
<She sounds like she was quite the priestess.>
"Izmir was. She was your grandmother. But if there was a flaw to her, it was that she did not trust. She never entrusted her daughter Lydia with the skills of the priestess, because Izmir never imagined we would lose. Without her, Phalos fell. We were forced to retreat into the caves and forests beyond. She was great, but she was only one. Since the empire had no use for a woman with a mind harder than stone, they killed her in a bombardment that slipped between her defense of leaves. But Izmir's daughter, Lydia, was only a child, and that was someone they could mold to their will, or so they thought. The emperor had never seen such power like the priestess's and wanted it for himself. So he kept Izmir's only daughter locked away on the Edict. It has taken this long to find those who were sympathetic to us, to give Lydia the coordinates, to tell her which escape pod she needed."
They reached the settlement. It was deep within the forest, away from the prying eyes and satellites of the empire. Ashra slid off of the spider's back and landed in a clearing. A fire had been built, its glow getting brighter as twilight faded to night. Zava's eyes could see clearly in the darkness, and she noticed the other climbers had similar, luminescent eyes.
Curious onlookers hidden in the trees looked at their guest. Ashra was the only one of them that was fully clothed, and she sat with a dignity that the others didn't understand. But after a while, she relaxed. The creases that lined her forehead smoothed out when she heard the singing and laughter in the forest. The climbers and runners danced around the fire, and the smell of roasting food made her mouth water.
Their plates were broad, waxy leaves and their utensils were their fingers or claws. Ashra had never eaten with her hands before, but she kept silent, watched, learned, and mimicked. The food was plain and delicious, rough in the way the ground was. There was no special preparation or techniques used to cure the meats or to turn the berries into a sauce. The food was simple and the company was superb. Ashra wondered if this was what her mother ate before she was exposed to the trappings of the empire. Aboard the Edict, where everything was elegant. Where the food may have been hot but the meal was cold.
<What do you call this?> she asked Orana, waving around a spiced strip of meat.
"Kallum. Large but fast, with tender meat. Yiz, a runner over there, found a large one that should keep us fed for awhile."
<What's a runner?> Ashra asked. She wanted to know about the world she was on. She felt at home. The hint of an idea appeared, nothing more than an image of her staying here for a long time.
"A better question is, what are we all?" Orana responded. "We were once fully human, all of us. We walked on two feet and ate with hands, not claws." Her red eyes looked away as she recalled the past. "Once the empire had Lydia, they experimented here. They were fascinated by the priestess line, and they marveled at the genetic diversity between us and the rest of the planet. The empire has always been fond of biologics." She picked up a blade of grass. "After Lydia was taken away," she continued, "they released mutagen gasses into the places where we hid. The children were affected first," she said, pointing to Zava and the other climbers. "We kept them deep in the caves where the cimmerians, the lizards that bask in the dark, also hid. As a result, their fingers changed to claws and their feet became scaled and their eyes found their power to see in the night."
She pointed to the runners. "They are the warriors who tended the ground birds, the katras, we kept for battle. Nowadays, they have less to fight, so they took to exploring and mapping the land. They are swift and act as scouts, keeping us aware of the few Sanctum that remain here. As for myself?" she paused. "I did not like spiders when I was younger, but I have come to appreciate my other half."
<What the empire has done is inexcusable.>
"We weren't people in their eyes. We were the unwanted results of their experiments. Their greatest triumph on Phalos was the arboreals, trees combined with the haga, great beasts that even we could not tame. Though in the end it seemed only their teeth remained inside the trees."
Despite her words, she looked proudly at the assembled group. "We have come to accept who we've become," she said. The others murmured their agreement. "And we are left alone. The Sanctum are still afraid of treading outside of their outpost, lest they get turned into something else." She and the others laughed. "You needn't worry child, the mist they used has long since disappeared and nothing has changed since we became who we are."
The night drew on. As the flames died, each person went their own way to find a place to sleep. But Ashra, her mind too busy with what she learned and the questions she still had, found herself gravitating towards Orana, who had walked off into the night. The maiden had given Ashra permission to use her eyes. Ashra walked beside her up a steep trail, seeing the world as she saw it.
They had come to the edge of a cliff that overlooked the forest they came from. The earth was cool and the night was warm. Both moons of Phalos hung brightly in the sky, casting their soft light, making the tops of the trees seem like a sea unto itself. Orana extended a hand. "May I?" she asked.
Ashra found the pendant and handed it to her. Orana held it up to the moonlight. "I had hoped," she said, "hoped for many years. But never did I think I would see the three moons of Phalos together again." The pendant seemed to capture the moonlight, the facets of the jewel absorbing it and taking its color for its own. She gave it back to Ashra.
"The Moon of Phalos, the one you hold, is the symbol of who we used to be. Only those who earned the approval of the slumberer could wear it."
<The slumberer?>
"She sleeps, but when the next priestess is named, she will awaken."
<I don't remember my mother ever wearing this,> she said.
"Your mother was never a priestess. She never took the journey to meet the slumberer. She was too young, and Izmir never let her. Now, you are the only one who can."
<How do I go on this journey?>
"The journey was never talked about. It was a process only Izmir knew, and perhaps Lydia, if she told her. If you don't know, it's a mystery and is lost to us now."
Ashra listened and wished her mother was there to explain to her what this all meant. But she put those thoughts aside and continued gazing through Orana's eyes at the sea of moonlight. The maiden was staring at a tower in the distance, one that rose above the forest, and so Ashra had no choice but to look at it as well.
<What is that?> she asked.
"The tower is part of the Sanctum outpost here. After they took your mother and the arboreals, there was no more need for military here. Without Izmir, we certainly didn't pose a threat to the empire. So the Sanctum departed, taking their ships and their plasma with them, and left a Marquis in charge with a group of men. That tower is the empire's remaining presence on Phalos."
To Ashra it seemed like a scar upon the landscape, a monolith out of place and out of time. Its spire blocked a part of one of the moons, and Ashra could only think of how beautiful it would be if it were gone.
<What have you tried?> she asked.
"Many things. But after Izmir passed and Lydia was taken?not many of us survived. What you see is the handful of children that made it. There were opposite results, where animals were merged with parts of human bodies. They were tormented beings, and it was unconscionable to force them to live in a body they couldn't control."
<The empire has done horrible things,> she said, and she thought of the reason behind her own birth.
"They have. We are weak, but if you are willing to fight them, we will rally behind you, as we did Izmir." She paused for a moment, hesitant to continue. "However, everyone only sees your mother in you. It seems I am the only one who remembers it takes two to make a child."
This was the conversation Ashra feared. She chose her words carefully. <I have never seen my father, and we have never spoken.>
"Yet you share his blood. They were only rumors Ashra, but he has done the same to other worlds. He would never merge with Lydia, or any woman, yet he still needed one who had her latent abilities. Someone he could control. You are a princess, and you are important to him. He will come looking for you, if he hasn't already."
<Who I am is not important to him. We have never spoken, and he has never shown me any affection. My time aboard the Edict was spent with my tutors and my mother. And even then, the time spent with my mother was limited. I know now she could not speak of things she wanted to speak of.>
"You do honor to your mother, but this," she said, encompassing the wide plain in the span of her palm, "is also your father's work. Can you turn against him so easily?"
<I was never his to begin with. I was born only as a tool, an instrument for the royal family to use. Even as a child I understood this, when I was isolated, when I was shunned, when love was denied to me by a man I wanted to love. But a tool can be used by anyone, even itself.>
"If you are decided, then we can find a way for you to control your power. The hair normally becomes white only when you have made the journey," she said, running her fingers through Ashra's hair, "but perhaps the slumberer granted you her approval even before your birth." The maiden's voice became stronger. "We shall reclaim Phalos, and you shall be the one who did it. We will sing of your deeds around the evening fires, if there are any of us left to sing."
They were quiet as they gazed out over the forests and hills. One of the moons was setting and the other followed. The maiden bowed once, as deep as her waist would allow. She turned and headed back to the settlement. Though Ashra no longer had sight, she felt the moonlight on her skin and wondered if her mother had seen the same moon, if she had ever stood on the same cliff Ashra was standing on now.
That night, she dreamt of a seed waiting to bud. A voice said, "You have returned," but she would not remember by morning.

Chapter 30

The Marquis d'Avester slapped his neck. His fingers came back covered in his own blood, along with bits and pieces of whatever insect it was that had been drinking it. Three more flew in to replace the one he smashed.
"Killian!" His voice echoed down the hall past the banquet table at which he sat alone. In response, he heard strong and even footsteps as Killian appeared. The soldier-turned-assistant entered the dining room and stood with his back straight and his arms at his sides. A scar ran down from his left eyebrow to his lip, cutting across what once might've been an attractive face. His body was lean and toned, in stark contrast to the marquis that sat in front of him. "Marquis," he said.
"I told you that I didn't want to be disturbed."
"Marquis, I-" Killian started, but the marquis silenced him with a raised hand.
"I am disturbed! I am disturbed every moment here. I am supposed to be enjoying breakfast by myself, but these?" he sought the right word but never learned the names of the insects of Phalos, "things?they've sucked more blood out of me than my last three wives combined! Why is that?"
"Marquis," Killian said patiently, "there seems to be a gap in one of the screens. It is being repaired. However, the-"
"No excuses! If I can't enjoy my morning, then no one will!" 
In name, he was the Marquis d'Avester, administrator of Phalos. But what was Phalos? With the exception of the outpost he and the other soldiers lived in, everything else was nature. Nature, that found its way through the concrete wall that surrounded the outpost; that found its way through the windows and onto his skin and into his blood. He was the marquis of useless land, of irradiated zones that everyone, including himself, was afraid to enter.
"No shields," the marquis said. "We've been reduced to common screens-"
"Marquis!" Killian said, unable to mask his contempt. "Seth, First Prince and Voice of the Empire, requests your time."
"The prince?"
"Yes, Marquis." Killian left a small device, no larger than a token, on the table.
The marquis made some buzzing noises and waved Killian away with a shaking of his wrist. Killian left, muttering something under his breath, but the marquis didn't have time to care.
He smoothed his hair and took a deep breath, but before he was ready, Seth's disembodied head appeared, projected from the device.
The marquis jumped out of his seat and fell over backwards. "My Lord," he said, his voice faint as it came from the floor. He scrambled to find his chair, but the cold stare in the prince's eyes made him stop and remain prostrate on the ground. Behind Seth's head were brief glimpses of dark walls lined with books, and the marquis guessed he was on Sanctuary.
"Is this a bad time?" Seth asked, the sarcasm dripping like venom. The prince had heard everything.
"No, no, My Lord. My time and the whole of my being belong to the empire."
"And your dedication?"
"My dedication is unwavering, and I seek only to fulfill your wishes upon Phalos."
"Then why is it you ignored a mandate I distributed across the empire?"
"Mandate, My Lord?"
"Certainly you saw it, if you are as vigilant as you say you are."
Suddenly the marquis wished he hadn't dismissed Killian. "I?I have not seen the mandate, Sire. Perhaps the transmission was lost in dark-"
"No more excuses. When did it arrive?"
The marquis's palms were sweating. He wished he knew the right response, but he drew a blank. He weighed lying to the First Prince against exposing his incompetence, and knew there was not a correct answer. So he remained silent.
"An object entered Phalos's atmosphere at 1137 universal time yesterday morning," Seth explained. "Despite your negligence, the system logged the entry and it was reported to me. Take your men, find where it crashed and record everything you see. If there are any survivors, arrest them and bring them to the outpost."
"My Prince, what should we be looking for?"
"An escape pod registered to the Edict."
"And what kind of survivors are we expect-"
"Just a young girl," The prince said, cutting him off. "The rest is none of your concern."
"But My Lord, we don't have the resources we used to on Phalos. The men are afraid of the irradiated-"
"Hold your tongue before I cut it out and make you eat it. If you wish to remain marquis, do as I have commanded. Your sensors have been calibrated to be sensitive to the waves we are looking for. Report every hour with your progress." 
Seth thought for a moment. "If you do well, you may become marquis of another world, one with fewer pests," he added as an afterthought. After one final glare, Seth's enlarged face disappeared.
The Marquis d'Avester waited another twenty seconds, a lifetime it felt, for the First Prince to become occupied by something else. He then called for Killian, the leader of the Sanctum troops on Phalos.

Chapter 31

"I was not a priestess so I cannot tell you what you are supposed to do," Orana said, standing next to Ashra. "But, I can share with you what I know."
Light from the setting sun filtered through the leaves. They were alone in a glade outside of the settlement. But on Phalos, where the trees have eyes and the wind carries conversation far, they were never truly alone. Zava looked out from the tallest branches, while the runners patrolled the edges of the forest. When they had heard that Ashra, daughter of Lydia, would join them in overtaking the Sanctum on Phalos, they found the budding seed of hope in their hearts. They vowed to keep her safe as she tread the path her mother didn't have a chance to walk.
<I have felt the tree,> Ashra said. <The first time was when my mother died, and I don't remember how it came about. The second time was on the Aurelius, when those men came after me. But I don't know how I did it. It seemed like someone else, someone separate from me, brought those blooms to life.>
"We call the light trees you mention the drasil," Orana said. The first priestesses brought their drasils forth when they were threatened. Phalos is a dangerous place, Ashra, and the animals hunt at all hours of the day and night. But sometimes worse than the animals were the men, and it was only after the first priestess awakened and destroyed her attackers that Phalos became a matriarchy." She chuckled at the tale. "The priestesses harnessed their own fear and used it. Your grandmother, Izmir, was the greatest we had ever known. She could command a divine wind to blow the leaves of her tree far into the sky where the Sanctum ships hid. Moments later, those ships would come crashing down as the leaves tore through their metal with nothing but light."
Ashra walked to the center of the glade. <I will make her proud,> she said.
"The drasil is rooted in death, which is rooted in life. It takes the essence of the being, its complete existence at the moment of death, and harnesses it, changes it to another form. The tree of every priestess is different, but they say it is the one that accompanies them in their mind; the one that is always with them."
Ashra removed herself from Orana's eyes and returned to the Empyrean. She saw the tree, her drasil, and willed its existence to leave her world, to be more than something only she could see. She tried to see it out upon the grass, tried to imagine its branches waving in the breeze. She checked with Orana's eyes, but there was nothing in the glade. The drasil remained fixed in her mind.
"For us, death is only the beginning," Orana said. "I was told that Izmir used a moment to create her drasil. The moment when life ceases to be, the moment when all of who one was, is."
Ashra remembered the two times she had brought her drasil forth. The first was with the death of her mother. She did not remember it well, but the second time, with the man she had slashed with the knife; that time she remembered. It had been a reflex, but as his life was drained by Aki's poison, she took what remained of his star and shaped it into a tree, the most familiar object to her and her only constant companion.
<But Maiden Orana, surely you don't mean I sacrifice a human to do it. The price is too high.>
"When the empire came with their fire from the sky, Izmir took our dead and raised them to great heights. With each one of us that fell they lost three. But she used death in all its forms. From the crushed trees to the cimmerians in their caves, each life became a part of the trees she created. Find death in all its places, even amongst the insect husks and yellowed grass, and you should have enough to start," Orana said.
Ashra looked in the sky of the Empyrean, at the darkness between the nebulas, at the stardust she had not bothered to consider. She held her hand like a net and wove her fingers through the dust, picking up the particles and gathering them until they formed a clump in her hand. She let the clump go, watched it sink under the island, under the water, but even then she felt its presence, as if it were another limb.
She danced. She moved her body to the simple rhythm of the songs she heard in the evening. She danced the way she saw Zava dance around the fire. As she drew her arms up she felt the clump sprout. She couldn't see it in the Empyrean, but as she pounded her feet to a rhythm in her mind, she felt it grow. It was no different than the way she could feel her arm lengthen when she stretched. She knew it took root in the real world, where the maiden was.
Where the maiden was.
She stopped dancing and sought out Orana, afraid of what she had done. She found her outside the glade, under the shadow of leaves. With the maiden's eyes, she saw herself in the center of the glade and the tree of light that she had only heard of and felt, until now. The branches sprouted above her head, but didn't reach much higher than that. 
<Maiden Orana, I am glad you had the sense to leave. But, if you would come closer?there's something here I'd like to see.>
Orana bowed and moved closer. Ashra looked at the drasil through borrowed eyes. Under the light, deep veins of black pulsed through the trunk and into its leaves. She felt each pulse inside her own body and noticed it matched the beating of her heart.
They were not the only ones to see it. Insects, drawn to the strange light, flew to it. One by one, they disappeared into its trunks and leaves, their bodies reduced to nothing. Ashra felt the essence of the tree grow. She danced and shaped it further. A stray bird dove for the insects and was absorbed as well. The influx of energy allowed her to lengthen the top so that it peeked out above the other trees.
Maintaining the drasil took all of her focus. Sweat broke out on her forehead. A wave of exhaustion overcame her and her eyelids felt heavy, as if she had been awake for days. Even in the Empyrean the stars seemed dimmer. When they were about to fade, she let the tree go. She collapsed onto the grass, breathing heavily.
The darkness within the veins of the tree stopped, freezing mid-pulse, fading to white. Then, the light itself fell apart, first into rays, then into motes, then into nothing. The tree disappeared.
Two spider legs lifted Ashra up, followed by two human arms. "You will make a fine priestess," Orana said. "Even Izmir would've been hard-pressed to create such a large drasil from so little material."
<What shall we do now?> Ashra asked drowsily.
"You shall rest. No more can be done today," she said, looking at the setting sun. "I shall keep guard for the evening and we shall continue tomorrow. Perhaps with more practice you may even attract the attention of the slumberer." She smiled at Ashra, left her in the care of some runners, and disappeared into the forest around the glade. She wove a different type of strand from the spinnerets on her abdomen. These strands were strong and almost invisible. She wrapped them around her fingers and legs and crawled between the trees, leaving a trail of silk behind her. She crossed and recrossed them with threads she wrapped around different branches surrounding the glade. It was a complex web, but one she, or the spider attached to her, knew how to make. They would be covered with blood by night's end.

Chapter 32

Far to the east, the Marquis d'Avester was alerted to the spike in delta waves. He and the personnel posted to Phalos stood in the basement of the tower. The tower served as the antenna for the planet, receiving and processing all data.
Seth's mandate recalibrated the radar arrays scattered on and above Phalos. They pinpointed the location of the burst, about forty miles to the west.
"Visual confirmation?" the marquis asked.
"Spotted," responded Killian. "There are reports of unexplained light that peaked above the emergent layer of the forest in sector K-5."
"Very well. Take a squad and cover the area. Find out what landed there, what survived, and what the source of those waves was."
"Understood, Marquis." Killian was glad to be out of the outpost and back on the field again, but he knew that the others would not be so willing to go. Though no mutations had been reported after the initial bombardment, rumors and stories meant for the dead of night persisted, about men who lost half their body and woke up with six limbs instead of four, their teeth replaced by mandibles.
"Would the marquis be willing to entice the other soldiers to journey for the glory of the Sanctum?" Killian asked.
"The glory of the Sanctum should be enough," the marquis said. "But in light of the First Prince's commands, I will offer an addition ten thousand credits for each one that returns with whatever was in the escape pod." Any price was worth paying to get off this rock, the marquis thought.
"Yes sir." Killian bowed and left, eager to don his armor and weapons again. Ten thousand credits! Half a year's salary for one excursion. Now he would have an easier time finding the right men.
What he didn't know was, when the moons rose again, none would be able to claim it.

***

It felt good to be in uniform again. Killian had taken off the decorative clothes that the marquis preferred to see and donned the armor of the Sanctum. The navy blue would blend into the sunset as they started their trek out. He was covered head to toe with a skin-tight suit, useful for keeping away the bugs that would otherwise eat him alive before he made his way past the wall of the outpost. It did nothing against any irradiation that remained, but he told the men the suit protected them from whatever was in the air and he hoped they were dumb enough to believe him. On top of the suit were pieces of interlacing fitted armor and a battery pack, encasing him in a powered exoskeleton. He flexed his elbows and knees, feeling the hydraulics and electricity that bound the exoskeleton together respond to his movements. 
A helmet completed the armor set. A wire snaked out from the bottom and sought out the input for his neural interface at the base of his skull. He winced as it found it and plugged itself in. His heads up display flashed and activated. Numbers and graphs scrolled off to the side of his vision, showing his coordinates along with the heart rates and blood chemical levels of his squad mates. The concentration of delta waves appeared as a blue marker to the west. 
He checked the plasma on the pistol and noticed it hadn't been used in years. Hopefully it could still fire. He holstered it above his thigh into a compartment built into the armor. If their mission was to secure one person, they wouldn't need that much firepower, but if the prince was interested, then this was no ordinary person. Thinking it better to be safe, he also grabbed the plasma atomizer, a bulky weapon that looked like a portable cannon. It felt heavy on his back, but the suit compensated for the extra weight.
Within half an hour, Killian had assembled the men he needed. Even with the incentive, only six others were willing to go. But these six would be enough. Though it was hard to tell under the helmets, their faces had been mangled by years of mercenary work; there were more scars than skin. Their features were set in scowls, and a little mutation wouldn't change their appearance much. They had grown up on different worlds, but they each knew two things. The first was hardship from the beginning of their lives. The second was the importance of money, both earning it and spending it.
The seven of them strode out into the twilight, the dark blue of their armor taking on a red tint from the setting sun. Levdisks, round, gray, and flat, waited on the ground, each one as wide as a man's shoulders. Killian stepped on top of one, followed by the mercenaries behind him. Once his neural signature was verified, his feet were locked to the surface of the disk. Blue light rings along the disk's edge came to life, revolving slowly at first and then picking up speed. When they spun so quickly that the sides seemed to be white, the dust around the levdisks vibrated off and they lifted off the ground with their riders atop them.
They hovered a few inches above the dirt; the air underneath each disk was distorted by the propulsion waves. Killian did some calibration checks by moving his legs back and forth, then leaning his body to the left and right. "How're the levdisks feeling?" he asked his team. When he received six ready signals, he leaned forward on his levdisk and sped off through the gate and into the forest beyond.
It was getting dark, but the lights on their helmets and the glow given off by their disks illuminated the way. They skimmed across the tops of the tall grasses, flattening them as they made their way deeper through the trees.
Despite what others feared, Killian liked the air of Phalos. It was rich and earthy and full of life. He loved the way it smelled after the rain, loved the way he could see for miles from the tower with nothing to obstruct his view. In his childhood, he was surrounded by tall buildings and large cities; an environment, a station, built by man. He had volunteered for the position on Phalos after the empire's conquest of the territory; he just didn't know it would be as an assistant, or servant so he liked to think, to the marquis. The marquis didn't deserve his station, and if there were a chance to stage an accident, Killian knew he would be the first to make sure it happened. This may be a good opportunity, he thought. A child that the First Prince wanted would be no ordinary child.
The others were silent, so his thoughts kept him company as they traversed the land. The trees started to thin out until they disappeared and gave way to a field. The wreckage of the escape pod was visible even in the darkness. It was a blemish upon the landscape, round and metallic against the flat plain. It left dirt and burnt grass in the wake of its crash. Killian unbound his feet, jumped off the levdisk and landed in front of the pod. He peered inside. Upon seeing nothing, he put his hand on the red cushions and felt around to see if there was anything else. His fingers touched a smear of dried blood on the floor. It was old and cracked and he didn't know what to make of it. Hopefully it didn't belong to the passenger. Killian knew the prince would prefer his quarry alive.
"Whatever was in here is gone," he said. The others got off their levdisks. Afraid the propulsion would disturb any fragile tracks, they searched on foot. It didn't take long to find a trail leading further to the west. The footprints belonged to a small human. The distance between each step was short, as if the person who made them was hesitant, as if they didn't know the right direction to go. This was good. A child lost on Phalos should be easy to persuade, easy to capture. Perhaps he had packed too much firepower, Killian thought. He was afraid he might scare the child away.
The trail went deeper into the woods. They got back on their levdisks and followed it, making sure to stay to the sides of the path so they could follow the tracks. As they went closer to the source of the delta waves, one of the mercenaries noticed a change in the prints. There were many more here, some like those of birds, and another set that seemed like large spikes driven into the ground. Before he could tell the others, he saw the light from his helmet reflect thin filaments strung between the trees. And before he could react, he went through them.
His levdisk split into two pieces. The man was split into many more, as the razor thin and sharp strands cut cleanly through his skin, muscle and bone. The pieces of the levdisk spun away in separate directions, as did his body. In a jumble of chunks, it fell with wet thuds into the grass.
Killian only had a second to look back and see his squad mate fall to pieces before he saw a similar web blocking his path between two trees. He shifted the plasma atomizer from his back to his hands and squeezed the trigger. A jet of blue flame, crackling at the tips like a lightning storm, expanded in a cone in front of him. The plasma engulfed the threads, burning them to ashes. He brushed away the trailing filaments from his visor. They left small cuts on his armor, exposing the unpainted metal beneath.
On his display, another set of vitals dropped to nothing; there was a spike of adrenaline and then the mercenary's heart rate flatlined. What was happening? There were never any reports of survivors on Phalos. There had only been a few scattered tribes across its vast land, and they were supposedly eliminated by the mutagens. Killian was sure the marquis was ordered to exterminate any survivors after the empire acquired the arboreals. So who was doing this?
He spun his head around and the lights on his helmet illuminated a shape. The top half was human, and he glared at him with human eyes. But beneath his waist were two long scaled legs that looked like they belonged to a large bird. They pumped up and down like pistons and kept pace with Killian's levdisk. The runner leapt up. He was about to kick Killian in the chest, but the soldier had already pulled out his pistol and fired. A burst of plasma collided with the runner in midair in a bright splash of blue. The runner fell limp to the ground. Killian's nose was filled with the smell of burnt hair and charred flesh.
Killian adjusted the propulsion on his levdisk and shot up a few feet into the air. He tried to get an idea of what was happening through the foliage and darkness that blocked his view. White eyes peered at him through the leaves, tracking him as he dodged and weaved between the trees. 
Runners ran alongside the mercenaries, matching their speed. They jumped and kicked, keeping the Sanctum away from a small glade. But they were no match for the plasma that splashed on them. Many fell with holes burnt through their bodies. 
Killian looked past the trees to find out what was there, what it was they were guarding. What he saw stunned him. It was a beautiful woman, with hair so black it blended into the night. Her features were gentle but her lips were pulled back into a snarl. Her human half was nude, radiant in the moonlight, but below her navel was a spider with six red eyes. She covered much ground on her eight legs. A mercenary that had stepped foot into the glade found himself impaled on one of her fangs. He screamed as venom entered the wound, its acidic mix melting him from the inside out.
In her hands he saw the faint outlines of the threads, the same threads that sliced his squad mates into pieces. She wove them between her fingers. In the moonlight they seemed soft as they framed her face. But then she threw the strands to the far side of the glade and pulled them together with both her human and spider limbs. Killian only heard the sound of wet lumps falling, followed by another flat line on the side of his display.
She hadn't seen him yet. He floated away into the trees and then jumped from the levdisk. Its blue light disappeared as it floated idly next to him. He adjusted the density setting on the plasma atomizer and hefted the weapon in both hands. He diverted all the remaining plasma into the shot, and aimed at the six glowing eyes.
She stuck her fangs into another man. As he thrashed about, Killian pulled the trigger. The gun launched a lance of superdense plasma; a long blue ray surrounded by guidance rings that kept its flight stable.
It vaporized the first tree it came through. A wake of scorched earth trailed behind it as it entered the spider's head. A moment later it exited out its abdomen and disappeared into the trees.
